Preface 
 
This thesis is conducted at the Technical University of Denmark as the final project for the study 
programme: Master of Science in Architectural Engineering. It has been conducted in cooperation 
with the Department of Civil Engineering under the supervision of associate professor Luisa Giuliani. 
The thesis covers 2x30 ECTS points with the project period starting on January 22nd, 2018 and ending 
on June 22nd, 2018.  
Kgs. Lyngby, June 2018

==PAGE:7==
vi 
 
Abstract 
The building industry is currently evolving towards being a more data-oriented industry. In this 
context, 3D building models have become a substantial part of any construction project where 
Building Information Modelling (BIM) is used to store and manage data for the project. The stored 
data is utilised by the different fields of expertise within the building industry either via integrated or 
external software. Structural fire design is, however, missing an integration with the BIM 
environment due to it being based on conventional, theoretical verification procedures. Bridging the 
gap by developing an integration to the digital data stream for structural fire design has been the focus 
for the content of this thesis. 
The approach to solve this has been to develop a design and verification software, SteFi, which 
performs calculations of steel beams and columns exposed to different fire conditions. The software 
is developed as a stand-alone software with a Graphical User Interface (GUI) and is based on the 
Python coding language. The program provides an option for importing element data from a BIM 
model and exporting the fire design results back into the model. For this purpose, Autodesk Revit 
2018 has been the focal point for this integration, to test the use of SteFi together with this widely 
used BIM software. Additionally, the visual programming extension, Dynamo 1.3.2, has been used 
to handle the extraction, export and import of data from and to the 3D model. Tests of the program 
have been conducted which demonstrates that it performs calculations fast and with an accuracy 
varying between 0.26% and 5.36% based on comparisons with known analytical results. 
A case study has been conducted with the developed software to investigate and compare the impact 
the different parameters for the fire design process have on the insulation design against the prescribed 
procedure stated in EN1993-1-2 and to showcase the software’s usability. The investigated 
parameters include the fire type, method for determining deterioration factors, imperfection factor, 
and calculation method which reveals that massive savings can be achieved. The impact has been 
measured as the difference in required amount of insulation needed to resist a certain fire. Changing 
the fire model from the commonly used ISO834 Standard fire curve to the Danish Parametric fire 
curve, resulted in savings of approximately 58% on the required amount of box encased gypsum 
board insulation for the investigated case structure, in the favour of the DS Parametric fire curve. 
Furthermore, the combined effect of using an alternative design method, contrary to the one provided 
by EN1993-1-2, results in savings of approximately 41%.

Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
1 
 
1 Introduction 
 
The present digital world and society is based on gathering and storing large amounts of data within 
various industries. The building industry is developing with fast pace towards being more data-
oriented, using end-to-end engineering and digital fabrication from 3D models described by the 
industrial revolutionary term: Industry 4.0 (Brettel, et al., 2014). Within this development in the 
building industry, the use of Building Information Modelling (BIM) have become more and more 
important in building design and construction. It calls for an inclusion of an increasing amount of 
architectural- and structural design data to be handled inside the BIM models. Especially the field of 
structural fire design is missing a platform for integration. Closing the gap between the conventional, 
theoretical methods of verification and the modern BIM environment, could provide early feedback 
in the design process and thereby enhance the structural design. 
 
1.1 Motivation 
Several governing instants have pushed for an increased use of BIM on public projects, and in 
Denmark the department of construction have used the ICT-agreement from 2007  (Bygningstyrelsen, 
2017) to do so. This document has been continuously updated and forms the basis on which all digital 
information for a public project of a given financial size must be organised. The initiatives have 
worked as intended, with BIM now being a natural part of any construction project. A specific 
example of an integration with a BIM environment is the use of Sigma which is a software database 
for cost estimations (Sigma Estimates, 2018). This software can be integrated with Autodesk Revit, 
a BIM software, where quantity take-out from the model can be sent to Sigma for cost calculations. 
This clearly indicates that BIM models can be used for other purposes than for example visualisations 
and drawing generations, if the right extensions and tools are used and developed. 
The increased use of BIM has flooded the market with many different software packages, which can 
make it a difficult task to find the right one to use (Abanda, et al., 2015). Within the fire engineering 
industry, this has been attempted to be eased by the DIFISEK+ project (Dissemination of structural 
fire safety engineering knowledge), where in part 4 (Wald, et al., n.d.), the available fire engineering 
software have been mapped and categorised to help disseminate when and to what purpose a given 
==PAGE:20==
 
2                                                         Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
software can be useful. The DIFISEK+ project is a follow-up on the DIFISEK project (CORDIS, 
2005), which served the purpose of gathering and spreading knowledge of fire engineering.  
In part 4 of the DIFISEK+ project, a team of professors from different universities in Europe have 
worked to categorise all available programs related to their use in fire engineering design. They 
investigated different parameters e.g. user friendliness and reliability of calculation in more detail for 
a few of the publicly available programs. There is no investigation of the ability and degree of 
integration to BIM programs, which indicates that fire safety engineering tasks are unrelated to BIM 
programs. Several articles (Li, et al., 2014) (Jeong & Lee, u.d.) (Yan-Chyuan Shiau & Chong-Teng, 
2013) suggests otherwise, but most work related to BIM and fire safety are for person safety and not 
related to structural fire safety and design. From the list of structural fire engineering software 
investigated in part 4 of the DIFISEK+ project, only one software, ELEFIR-EN, capable of verifying 
steel structures, is publicly available, though only in a demo version and with no BIM integration at 
all. 
 
1.2 Problem Statement 
This thesis is aimed at closing the gap between conventional structural fire design methods and the 
BIM environment by developing software for fire design of structural steel elements that can be fully 
integrated to a BIM platform. Despite that structural fire safety engineering software already exists, 
developing a new program from scratch allows for a better integration to BIM as this will give full 
control and flexibility of the entire process from data import, performing calculations, to data export. 
By creating a stand-alone calculation software, licences to specific BIM software are not needed 
which is a common issue for many who works with BIM (Gjelsted, 2017). The software should be 
publicly available with user-friendliness as a priority and provide fast and accurate calculations, 
whilst being able to interact with data embedded in the BIM models.  
With the developed software, the prescriptive design approach from Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 
2007) is to be compared with alternative calculation procedures to verify and showcase the usage of 
the software and to investigate the impact of the alternative calculation procedures. The software 
should contain options for designing and verifying with both standard fire exposure and parametric 
fire exposure.  
 
 
==PAGE:21==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
3 
 
An investigation of the widely used resistance class design method, using standard fire exposure, is 
to be conducted since it is deemed to be unsafe based on its time of origin and basis of development 
(Ariyanayagam & Mahendran, 2013). The results of this investigation are expected to give indications 
on possible savings in the required amount of insulation needed to protect a steel structure exposed 
to fire. 
 
 
 
 
OBJECTIVES 
• Implement structural fire design in a BIM environment through software development 
• Ensure fast and accurate calculations 
• Investigate the conventional resistance class design method and its applicability 
==PAGE:22==
 
4                                                         Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
2 Method 
 
This thesis has mainly been conducted by the means of literature studies, to clarify differences in fire 
design theories and to investigate different possibilities for the software development. Additionally, 
a case study has been conducted to test the actual appliance of the developed software and investigate 
different fire exposure scenarios. Both methods will be described in the following sections. 
 
2.1 Literature Study 
Gathering information from different sources to emphasise the problem statement and the verification 
theory is as important as the development itself. This search for information has also been crucial for 
the initial phase of the development to enhance a limited amount of previous experience with software 
coding. 
 
2.1.1 Steel Verification Methods 
The procedure for verifying steel structures exposed to fire is developed based on the well-known 
verification of elements at room temperature (20 °C). However, different methods for this verification 
are found within the literature where the approach can vary from method to method. This is not an 
immediate problem, but it is important to consider the different approaches and choose the most 
suitable one. 
In Denmark, the verification of steel structures exposed to fire should follow the Building Regulations 
BR18 (Trafik-, Bygge- og Boligstyrelsen, 2018) which refers to Eurocode 1 with modifications by 
the National Annex. However, it might seem fit to apply different methods since Eurocode only 
explicitly states simplified calculation procedures with the use of standard fire curves but allows for 
the use of parametric fire curves if more advanced calculations are conducted. Advanced calculation 
methods should include element analysis (limit analysis) of beams and consider the effect of hindered 
thermal expansion for columns. Therefore, to include these aspects, the verification theory presented 
in the Swedish literature (Pettersson, et al., 1976) has also been applied, with addition from the 
method for determining material deterioration presented by Hertz (Hertz, 2007).  
 
 
==PAGE:23==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
5 
 
2.1.2 Software Development 
The development of the software is separated into two parts, one concerning a calculation module, 
and one concerning a BIM integration module. Creating an independent calculation module, gives 
the possibility to have a very general calculation procedure which takes the same type of input in all 
cases. This makes it possible for the calculation module to interact with various BIM programs, hence 
allowing a more versatile use of the developed software.  
By developing an external calculation module, the transfer of data between the calculation module 
and the BIM program needs to be simple and general to ensure that different BIM programs can write 
identical files. The BIM integration module will be specific to each BIM program since the data 
structure varies from program to program (Porcari, et al., 2015), but the data needed for export should 
be so general that it is reasonable to assume that it can be extracted from the BIM program. The 
integration will, in short terms, consist of an add-on export/import module to a BIM program, which 
can export data to a file format readable by the calculation module and vice versa. 
Interacting with the developed software should be easy and intuitive so it goes along with the visual 
oriented BIM environment. It is therefore reasonable to consider using a Graphical User Interface 
(GUI) as the main “communication”/platform for the user. A GUI can in general be described as an 
on-screen interface that can be interacted with to execute different commands. Many applications use 
a GUI to ease the user’s experience with the program instead of only working with code being 
executed in the “background” or via command prompts.   
 
Calculation Module 
Choosing a programming language for the development of the calculation module, depends on 
various parameters, including how familiar the developer is with the different languages. The goal is 
to choose the language that fits the best with the scope of the project, which is to develop a stand-
alone calculation program with a GUI that makes the program easy to use. With some investigation 
of different general programming possibilities, a table with pros and cons have been established. 
Various communities and blogs such as Scratch (Scratch, 2013) and Randstad (Randstad, 2017) 
describes the difference between general programming languages which have been used to generate

It is rather difficult to make an immediate conclusion from Table 2.1 to the extent of choosing a 
suitable programming language. In general, it seems that Java is the most commonly used language 
for application development with no clear disadvantages other than it being a difficult language to 
setup and learn. Together with the fact that no previous experience has been obtained, it becomes a 
less favourable choice. Instead, considering C# with its possibilities with drag-and-drop editors and 
easy compiling to .exe files, seems like a better choice. However, C# is built on the .NET framework 
which only allows the application to run on Windows OS and only with the correct version of .NET 
installed. This is a big disadvantage compared to some of the other languages which can run on most 
major operating systems.

7 
 
The easily readable syntax in Python makes it preferable when considering the possibility of further 
development of the program by others, combined with Python being the only language where 
previous experience has been obtained. Python is also chosen for its capability of running the 
application on other operating systems such as Linux and Mac OS.  
Python does not come with an initial GUI development kit, so an extension of the language is needed 
to build the calculation module. Tkinter (Tk) is the most commonly used GUI package for Python 
with a lot of different possibilities of making cross-platform GUI development (python, 2017), and 
is therefore chosen as the interface module. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
BIM Integration Module 
With Python as the coding language chosen for the calculation module, the choice of coding language 
for the BIM integration module could be made based on the same arguments. However, the BIM 
integration module needs to be specific for each BIM program, given the varying data structure. For 
some BIM programs, other coding languages might be a better fit than Python. The different ways to 
incorporate the BIM integration module must be based on the possibilities for each specific BIM 
program and not limited by the coding language. 
The most commonly used BIM program in Denmark is Revit from Autodesk (Gjelsted, 2017) and 
given prior experience with this program and knowledge of the data structure, this is the software 
chosen as the BIM program to create a BIM integration module for. The integration module is 
developed in cooperation with Lorenzo Beltrani, a graduate student working on a project at the 
Technical University of Denmark, with a scope somewhat similar to the scope of this thesis. Beltrani’s 
work is conducted with Revit 2018 as platform which gives the possibility for a cooperation on 
developing an integration module for this specific BIM program. 
With that said, within Revit 2018 there are multiple ways to create an export/import module. The 
different methods investigated for incorporating such a module into Revit each has pros and cons as 
listed in Table 2.2. 
Tk   &

As for the decision of coding language for the calculation module, it is difficult to decide on the best 
way to implement the BIM integration module, based on which method would work the best, since it 
depends on personal preference. Creating the BIM implementation module as a macro in Revit would 
require the least of the user, both when using the module and installing it since no additional programs 
needs to be installed other than the BIM integration module itself. It is the same case with Dynamo 
except that it has the possibility of using Python coding. Ultimately, the choice of platform for the 
BIM integration module was to use Dynamo, chosen in cooperation with Beltrani. 
When data from the BIM integration module and the calculation module is exchanged, it is either via 
an .xlsx file, the native Excel file format, or a .csv file. The choice of .xlsx as a file format is based 
on the familiarity most people have with Excel, and that it is easy to preview the data between export 
and import. Furthermore, the table structure Excel uses to organise data makes it easy for the user, 
when previewing the export file, to identify possible errors and act accordingly. The .csv file format 
is a commonly used format to exchange data and is supported by many applications. Compared to the 
.xlsx format, an export file in the .csv format can be more difficult to preview, given that the data is 
written in lines with the values separated by a comma. On the other hand, the simplicity of the format 
==PAGE:27==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
9 
 
is its strength and the reason why it is widely supported, whilst the .xlsx format is more complex. 
Together the two formats form a strong basis for the file import and export, with the possibility of 
interacting with many programs, and the possibility to easily preview the files. Additionally, the .csv 
format allows users without access to Excel to preview the import/export data. 
 
2.2 Case Study 
To analyse the differences found in the literature study of the steel verification methods and the 
applicability of the developed software, a case study has been conducted to showcase the findings 
and their impact. The chosen case is an uninsulated steel carpark erected in 2000 for performing full 
scale fire tests previously used as a case study by Iwona Budny (Budny, 2010), to compare European 
and Scandinavian regulations. The structure contains elements of multiple cross-sections and with 
different loads for both beams and column. This variety will provide a sufficient sample size for 
testing how the variations found in the literature impacts fire resistance of beams and columns.  
 
 
==PAGE:28==
 
10                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
3 Theory 
 
The sections in this chapter will describe the theory implemented in the calculation module for the 
design and verification of steel members. Each section contains an elaborated description of different 
topics within structural fire safety analysis. Firstly, a description of the fire development which results 
in elevated room temperatures and how this effects the steel. Elevated temperatures for the steel 
results in a change of properties for the steel and a verification can be made for both beams and 
columns. This verification theory is described for both the simplified calculation methods stated in 
Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007), and for an alternative verification procedure with more advanced 
calculation methods. This includes limit analysis of beams and element analysis of columns with 
effects from hindered thermal expansion (Pettersson, et al., 1976). 
 
3.1 Fire development 
When working with structural fire safety, we assume a post-flashover state of the fire to verify for the 
worst-case scenario, where a compartment is undergoing extensive heat effects. In the post-flashover 
state, a fire can be described with different fire curves depending on the compartment geometry, 
content, surface materials etc. This thesis will focus on the use of the ISO834 Standard fire curve 
(DS/EN 1991-1-2, 2007) and the DS Parametric fire curve which is chosen for the Danish National 
Annex to Eurocode 1 (EN 1991-1-2 DK NA, 2007). The reason for using a performance-based 
approach, with the parametric fire curves, is that the layout and materials of buildings have changed 
over the years and made the otherwise conservative standard fire curve less conservative. The ISO834 
Standard fire curve was developed in the early 1900s and may therefore not be appropriate for 
representing a fire development in a modern building (Ariyanayagam & Mahendran, 2013). 
The ISO834 Standard fire curve is given by: 
 
VK = 20 + 345 ⋅logYh(8_ + 1) 
(3.1)
The DS Parametric fire curve is given by:

3.2 Steel Heating 
As an effect of the increased temperature in a fire compartment the steel temperature will increase 
due to convection and radiation. The heat transfer from the gas to the steel is based on a heat balance 
equation which describes the increase in steel temperature in a time interval. This steel heating is 
described with similar notations both by Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) and in the Swedish 
literature (Pettersson, et al., 1976). The steel heating formulas are divided in two categories: 
uninsulated steel and insulated steel.  
 
3.2.1 Uninsulated

3.3 Material Deterioration 
Mild construction steel will, as almost any material, expand when exposed to higher temperatures 
from a given fire. This expansion results in a deterioration of the steel which will affect the yield 
strength and the stiffness of the material. The deterioration is described differently in literature, where 
the Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) has separate reduction factors for strength and stiffness, with 
values stated for a 100 °C stepwise increase with linear interpolation as method to find values in 
between the stated values. In the Swedish literature (Pettersson, et al., 1976), different reduction 
factors for strength and stiffness are given by graphs, but the reduction factors are different from the 
ones stated in the Eurocode. Finally, the deterioration can be described with an S-shaped curve 
derived by Kristian Hertz (Hertz, 2007) which has the mathematical definition: 
 
^*()) = _ +
1 −_

:

==PAGE:34==
 
16                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
with )(, )/, ):, )2O and _ being material constants, which for mild steel with 0.2 % stress, are 6000 
°C, 620 °C, 565 °C, 1100 °C and 0, respectively. The definition also covers the use of 2.0 % stress 
where the material constants in this case will be 100000 °C, 100000 °C, 593 °C, 100000 °C and 0, 
respectively. 
Figure 3.3 – Reduction factors for mild steel as a function of temperature 
The difference between the reduction factors are clear when plotted in the same graph as seen on 
Figure 3.4, but due to missing information regarding the formula for the deterioration factors used by 
Pettersson et. al., the curve has been approximated by measuring on Figure 3.3 (left).  As seen on 
Figure 3.4 the deterioration factor given by Hertz’ formula returns a larger stiffness reduction than 
both the Eurocode and the Swedish literature. The same is true for the strength reduction, although 
the strength reduction used by the Swedes, is very similar to the one given by Hertz. This indicates 
that the deterioration curves from Hertz’ formula results in more conservative reduction factors than 
both the Eurocode’s and Swedish’ ones.

Construction steel (mild steel) will regain its full strength when the steel temperature drops in the end 
phase of a fire (Hertz, 2007). Therefore, it is obvious that the verification of a steel element exposed 
to fire should be done at the time of maximum steel temperature, since this is the time where the steel 
will have the lowest strength and stiffness. 
 
3.4 Beam Verification 
The verification of steel beams can be done for both section failure and element failure (limit 
analysis). The section failure method is the one given by the Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) and 
should normally be used when verifying with a standard fire exposure. The element failure 
verification is a more advanced calculation based on a limit analysis of the element. Both methods 
will be covered in the following sections.  
 
3.4.1 Section Failure 
The method for determining the ultimate moment resistance of a beam section is in Eurocode 
dependent on the cross-section class. For beams with a uniform temperature distribution, the moment 
resistance is found as: 
 
abc,QS = _d,e ∙Lfg,8
fg,QS
P ∙abc 
(3.25)
where 
 _d,e is the strength reduction factor found by table 3.1 in (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007)  
fg,QS is the partial factor for the relevant material property for the fire situation, usually 
         equal to 1.0 
 
fg,8 is the partial factor for normal temperature 
abc is the plastic moment resistance for normal temperature design (a6h,bc) for cross- 
        section class 1 and 2, and the elastic moment resistance for normal temperature 
        design (aih,bc) for cross-section class 3 
 
The moment resistance of a beam with a class 4 cross-section is determined in the same way as for 
class 1, 2, and 3, but only if the steel temperature is less than the critical temperature. The critical 
temperature is by Eurocode recommend as 350 °C. 
 
The ultimate moment resistance of a beam section for normal temperature design can be found by 
multiplying the section modulus with the yield strength at 20 °C and dividing with the partial factor: 
==PAGE:36==
 
18                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
 
abc = j ⋅kd
fg,8
 
(3.26)
The section modulus is a geometrical property that can be divided in two categories: the elastic section 
modulus, jih, and the plastic section modulus, j6h. The relation between the two is used to determine 
the total plastic benefit of the section which is used for the limit analysis (see Section 3.4.2). The 
elastic section modulus can be determined by: 
 
jih = l
m 
(3.27)
where 
I is the moment of inertia 
 
y is the distance from the neutral axis to the outermost edge 
The plastic section modulus can be determined by Ehlers (Ehlers, 2009): 
 
j6h = 2 ⋅no 
(3.28)
where 
 no is the static moment for half of the section around either axis (neutral axis) 
Formulas for the plastic section modulus of three common profile types have been conducted and can 
be seen in Appendix A. 
The temperature for a given timestep (standard fire) or the maximum temperature for the fully 
developed fire (parametric fire) can now be used to determine the strength reduction of the steel. The 
reduction is used to calculate the moment resistance during the fire, abc,QS. This can be compared 
with the actual imposed moment on the beam based on the design load and boundary conditions. For 
the beam to be verified, the following should always be fulfilled: 
 
abc,QS ≥a*,QS 
(3.29)
where 
a*,QS is the imposed bending moment on the beam 
Alternatively, a design approach can be used where the calculation criteria is based on a given design 
load. If the moment resistance of the section is equated with the bending moment imposed on the 
beam, the following relation can be considered: 
 
a*,QS = abc,QS = _d,e ∙Lfg,8
fg,QS
P ∙abc 
(3.30)
==PAGE:37==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
19 
 
So, with information about the support conditions and the load on the beam (giving the imposed 
moment), it is possible to determine the reduction factor, _d,e and thereby the critical temperature of 
the steel, )IJ. This temperature can then be compared with the actual steel temperature for a given 
timestep (for standard fire) or for the maximum steel temperature of a fully developed fire. If this 
steel temperature is larger than the critical temperature, the section needs to be insulated. With a given 
maximum steel temperature, Equation (3.21) can be used to determine the required insulation 
resistance, RS/TS, with the possibility to determine both the needed thermal conductivity or the 
insulation thickness. 
 
3.4.2 Element Failure 
The limit analysis of a beam is based on obtaining a collapse mechanism by forcing the beam into a 
plastic state where plastic hinges occur due to a load higher than the yielding load. This will give us 
the limit load, qhSr, which also can be described by: 
 
qhSr = s ⋅qih 
(3.31)
where 
s is the total plastic benefit at 20 °C 
qih is the elastic load, determined from the maximum elastic moment (specified for each  
      static scheme) 
The total plastic benefit with no fire exposure is determined by: 
 
s = t ⋅u 
(3.32)
where 
t is the redistribution along the section (also called the shape factor) 
 
t = a6h
aih
= j6hkd
jihkd
 
(3.33)
 
u is the redistribution along the element 
 
u = qhSr
q(
 
(3.34)
where 
q( is the plastic load, determined from the maximum plastic moment (specified for each 
     static scheme) 
So, to determine the redistribution along the element we will need to calculate the limit load, qhSr, 
which is when enough plastic hinges occur for the element to become a mechanism. This should be 
done for any given static scheme, but for simplicity reasons it is chosen to focus on a handful of 
==PAGE:38==
 
20                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
different static schemes with different loading types. There are different ways for determining the 
limit load, where the basic procedure is to use the incremental analysis, by increasing the load in 
increments, resulting in plastic hinges occurring. Afterwards, the plastic moment for the static 
scheme, whenever a new plastic hinged is created, is determined. Superimposing the static schemes 
(or the plastic moments) will then give the limit load (Byskov, 2005). 
The other, and more direct way of finding the limit load, is to use the principle of virtual work, also 
done by (Byskov, 2005). This is done by equating the external work with the internal work of the 
beam e.g.: 
 
jv = jw 
(3.35)
As an example, Figure 3.5 illustrates the collapse mechanism of a simply supported beam with a point 
load in the middle. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The external work, jv, can in this case be determined as the force times the vertical movement of 
the plastic hinge: 
 
jv = x ⋅∆ 
(3.36)
where: 
 
∆= z
2 ⋅{ 
(3.37)
The internal work is found by multiplying the plastic moment with the number of created rotational 
angles for the plastic hinge, in this case 2{, thus: 
 
jw = a6h ⋅2{ 
(3.38)
Δ

mechanism

z  
(3.39)
The limit load is found for different static schemes, which can be seen in Appendix B. These values 
and schemes are the ones included in the calculation module for the fire analysis. With the limit load 
in place, the total plastic benefit can now be calculated. 
For the limit analysis we would also like to take into consideration the effect from the fire exposure. 
This is simply done by modifying Equation (3.32), by multiplying it with the deterioration factor 
given in Equation (3.24), thus: 
 
s()) = t ⋅u ⋅^*()) 
(3.40)
Inserting this into Equation (3.31) we get: 
 
qhSr()) = s()) ⋅qih 
(3.41)
The limit load found with Equation (3.41) should then, in the case of a verification procedure, be 
compared with the actual fire design load acting on the beam. Similar as for the section failure 
criterion, we can use the design approach if we impose qhSr()) = qQS: 
 
qQS = s()) ⋅qih 
(3.42)
where 
qQS is the fire design load on the beam 
From Equation (3.42) it is possible to determine the critical temperature, )IJ, because this will be the 
only unknown variable (if we have information about the load and boundary conditions of the beam). 
This critical temperature can, similar as for the sectional analysis, be compared with the maximum 
steel temperature gained from a given fire development and hereby design the insulation if needed. 
A comparison between the limit analysis method described in this section and the known data 
presented on graphs in the Swedish literature have been conducted to check whether the results are 
valid. This is done by calculating the plastic benefit s()) for different temperatures, using Equation 
(3.40) for three different static schemes; simply supported in both ends, fixed in both ends and simple 
in one end with the other fixed, all with uniform distributed loads. The comparison of the fixed end 
beam can be seen on Figure 3.6 while the other two can be found in Appendix C. 
==PAGE:40==
 
22                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
 
Figure 3.6 – Comparison of plastic benefit for rising temperatures (dashed line = calculated values) 
The three graphs represent different support conditions with varying plastic benefit as found when 
comparing the derived equations for the limit loads in Appendix B with the elastic load for the same 
static scheme. The comparison clearly shows a tendency for the Swedish curves to overestimate the 
plastic benefit in an area between 200 °C to 550 °C making the calculated values more conservative 
at any temperature step. The reason for this might be that the Swedish curves are generated with 
respect to the effect of heating and cooling, which is also the reason for the three different curves (I, 
II, III), while the calculated values are only dependent on the actual steel temperature. 
 
3.5 Column Verification 
The column verification can, like the beam verification, be divided into two types of analysis. One is 
using an element failure criterion based on the formulas stated by Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007). 
The other method considers the effect from thermal expansion resulting in indirect stresses in the 
profile (Pettersson, et al., 1976). Both methods will be covered in the following sections. 
3.5.1 Element Failure  
When verifying a steel column and determining its loadbearing capacity during fire, most often the 
column is verified without including the surrounding structure’s influence on the member. This leads 
to a verification of the column on an element level which according to Eurocode is sufficient if the 
==PAGE:41==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
23

difference for each steel strength (220, 260 and 320 MPa) is found to be -3.0 -3.9 and -4.5 MPa 
respectively. Given these relatively small differences, the deterioration factor found by Equation 
(3.24) is in combination with Equation (3.76) arguably a valid way of determining the reduced 
loadbearing capacity of a steel column. As the buckling stresses found using Equation (3.24) is lower

than the ones presented in the Swedish literature, using Hertz’ formula is on the safe side and a more 
conservative estimation. Furthermore, calculating the buckling stress instead of measuring on a graph 
eliminates all measuring errors, and allows for an infinite number of different steel stress types and 
degrees of hindered expansion to be calculated and not limited by the graphs given in the literature.

determined by:

3.6 Summary 
Comparing the verification procedures and formulas of the Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) with 
the ones stated in the Swedish literature (Pettersson, et al., 1976), reveals both differences and 
equalities. Especially the different ways of determining the deterioration of the steel have shown to 
be important to consider. The analytical method given by Hertz (Hertz, 2007) has shown to be more 
conservative as it considers 0.2 % stress, resulting in lower values of capacity of both beams and 
columns, and does give the possibility of determining the reduction without the use of interpolation 
between temperatures. 
Additionally, parameters such as the convective heat transfer coefficient, AI, and the emissivity differ 
between the Eurocode steel heating formulas for unprotected steel members compared to the method 
stated in the Swedish literature. Eurocode imposes the value of 35 W/(m2 °C) for the convective heat 
transfer coefficient while the Swedes suggests the use of 23 W/(m2 °C) for parametric fires. One can 
consider if the higher value should be used for any given parametric fire as it is more conservative. 
The comparison of the plastic benefit as function of temperature resulted in the calculated values 
always giving lower values than the graphs presented in the Swedish literature. The presented theory 
will be integrated in the software and is deemed to produce results on the safe side. 
Finally, the development of the buckling stress equation and comparison with the one given by 
Eurocode shows a difference in the way of determining the imperfection, where the Swedish method 
is non-temperature dependent and Eurocode is temperature dependent. Also, the comparison of the 
deterioration given by Hertz and the deterioration stated in the different literature, when looking at 
the difference in buckling stresses, results in a noticeable difference, where Hertz’ values ends up 
being more critical for temperatures above 300 °C. In addition to this, comparing results for the effect 
of hindered thermal expansion have shown to give very large differences between the method 
suggested in this thesis compared to the data from the Swedish literature. There is great a possibility 
that the theory used to include the effect of the indirect stresses differs between the two methods since 
this does not have an elaborated description in the Swedish literature. The Swedes states that their 
presented data is based on approximations and that they use the secant modules instead of the regular 
elastic modulus together with the 0.5 % stress, which of course affects the results when comparing 
the methods. Furthermore, a parameter such as the thermal expansion coefficient has not been 
described in the literature and may therefore also differ from the value used in this thesis. 
==PAGE:53==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
35 
 
4 Program Development 
 
This chapter will cover the work and development of both the calculation module and the integration 
module that in combination is considered as an automated and BIM integrated calculation procedure 
for fire design and verification of steel elements with focus on beams and columns. 
Not every line of code will be described, but highlights and crucial implementation will be described 
in the following sections. The entire source code for the program can be seen in the associated code 
report found in Appendix H. 
 
4.1 Calculation Module 
The module for running the verification or design of steel elements is named SteFi (Steel Fire design) 
and is created with the graphical user interface module Tkinter in the well-known Python coding 
language. The interface is created as a fixed size window with multiple tabs for navigating between 
content as seen on Figure 4.1. The program offers the possibility to perform either a verification, 
where the load-bearing capacity of an element is calculated or a design calculation where the required 
insulation thickness (if any) is determined based on a given load on the element.  
Cross-section data for an element is currently dictated by three pre-set types of sections (I-Profile, 
RHS and CHS) which are defined in the program by geometrical data such as width, height, thickness 
etc. The support conditions of an element are chosen in a similar way, by a total of eight pre-set 
support and load scenarios for beams with either a point load in the middle or a uniform distributed 
load, and three for columns. 
Parameters that can vary for different fire scenarios are in general possible to change by the user as 
well as input such as steel strength, stiffness, partial factor for fire scenario etc. However, one 
parameter has been fixed for simplification purposes, which is the parameter for choosing maximum 
axis range for time, when plotting fire- and steel heating curves. The result of this, is that the plot 
windows always will have identical axis ranges, so they are comparable for different analyses. 
==PAGE:54==
 
36                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
Furthermore, a limitation has been made to the possible fire exposure scenarios, which currently can 
be set as either full exposure on all sides, exposure on three sides with coverage on top (if beam) or 
either parallel to strong axis or weak axis for columns. 
 
 
Figure 4.1 – SteFi interface tabs
==PAGE:55==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
37 
 
4.1.1 Project Files 
The code for the calculation module is divided into three separate Python files; a front-end file 
(SteFi.py), a back-end file (BackEnd.py) and a report-file (Report.py) for generating a report of the 
results. The front-end file contains all the lines of code necessary to run the graphical interface, 
plotting graphs, import/export etc. The back-end file contains definitions with the needed formulas 
from the theory to perform the calculations. Both files are structured in a way where various 
definitions (functions) are created to give a simplistic overview of the files. Having the definitions 
for the calculation placed in its own file results in a collection of generic calculation definitions which 
can be distributed to other projects if needed. The file for generating the result reports is created in a 
separate file, mainly because it contains many lines of code, is somewhat generic, and can be used 
for other purposes. Additionally, the report file is separated from the back-end file since it serves a 
completely different purpose and might be used in other cases different to the cases where the back-
end would be used. 
 
 
 
 
 
Definitions within the back-end file are called individually in the front-end file when they are needed 
for a certain calculation. The output (or so-called return function) of the definitions are then either 
used directly in the overall calculation or stored in a global interface variable which will be described 
in the following section.  
Besides the three Python files, containing the overall code for the program, various picture files are 
found within the project folder. These picture files are used by the program to illustrate things like 
cross-section type, boundary conditions and fire exposure type. 
 
4.1.2 Declaring Variables 
When coding in Python, a commonly used element is a variable which holds information about either 
integers, floating numbers or strings (text). Variables can be called in functions, loops, if/else 
Figure 4.2 – Code structure and data flow
Report
Plots
BackEnd.py
SteFi.py
Report.py
Data export 
GUI
Data import
==PAGE:56==
 
38                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
statements etc. using the variable’s name, which is usually declared before running the function, like 
seen in the example code below: 
Code snippet 1 – Basic Python variable syntax 
1. x = 2  
2. y = 2*x   
3.    
4. print(y) 
>>> 4 
 
Here, x is the variable which is used to determine another variable, y. This method of declaring 
variables is quite intuitive and gives a linear approach when the code is initiated and runs through 
each line. However, when working with a GUI this method for declaring variables is a bit different. 
The GUI is dynamic in the sense of giving the user an opportunity of changing input data. This can 
for example be done by placing an Entry widget, which is Tkinter’s way of creating interactable input 
fields. 
 
 
  
This kind of input field can be changed by the user and the associated variable in the code needs to 
follow this input. For this to work in the Tkinter module, an empty variable must be declared initially. 
This is either done for an integer, a floating number or a string by calling the syntax IntVar(), 
DoubleVar() or StringVar() respectively, as also seen in Code snippet 2. 
Code snippet 2 – An example of declaring variables using Tkinter module 
1. # Declaring variables   
2. H = IntVar()      # Height   
3. B = IntVar()      # Width   
4.    
5. # Creating user input fields (entries)   
6. height_entry = ttk.Entry(root, textvariable=H, width=5)   
7. width_entry = ttk.Entry(root, textvariable=B, width=5) 
 
The two integer variables for height and width are in this example assigned to the entries and will 
therefore change according to the user input of those entries.  
Now, if we would like to access the value inherited in those variables, after an input is given by the 
user, we cannot simply use the syntax as shown in Code snippet 1, to perform an operation to that 
Figure 4.3 – Example of two Entry widgets for 
a height and width parameter 
==PAGE:57==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
39 
 
variable. It is instead necessary to get or set the value of the variable by calling the syntax from the 
Tkinter module as seen in Code snippet 3. 
Code snippet 3 – Setting and retrieving variable’s values with the Tkinter module 
1. x = IntVar()   
2. x.set(2)   
3.    
4. print(x.get()) 
>>> 2 
In this case, the x variable is initially made as an empty integer variable, then set equal to 2 and finally 
printed by using x.get(). Knowing the difference between the two methods of declaring variables is 
essential for the program to run when using the Tkinter module. 
All variables associated to entry fields are in SteFi set as string variables. This is not very intuitive 
when reading through the code, but the result of doing so is that the program will contain entry fields 
that are blank when it is opened for the first time. If the variables were set as integer or double 
variables the fields would instead consist of either 0 or 0.0 respectively. The reason for having the 
entry fields blank is that the user will not mistakenly think that a given parameter should have the 
value of zero. So, the user is now obligated to check and apply data to all the blank entry fields, 
whereas some might have default pre-set values.  
 
4.1.3 Interface Layout 
We can now consider the general layout of the interface and how the user interacts with the different 
widgets (buttons, entry fields, dropdown menus etc.). Before these different widgets can be placed, a 
basic empty window needs to be initiated by calling the code shown in Code snippet 4.

1. from tkinter import *

==PAGE:58==
 
40                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
Code snippet 4 shows the very basis of the front-end file and generates a basic window as seen on 
Figure 4.4. In line 1, the Tkinter module is imported with all the content available in that specific 
module indicated by the “*”. In line 4, the window is created and named root. Whenever root is 
referred later in the code, for example when placing widgets, it means that a given widget is placed 
in the main window. Through line 7 to 9 various settings for the window is given such as the title and 
size and whether the window can be resized or not. The last line in Code snippet 4 merely ensures 
that the window does not close after all previous lines of code has been read, but stays in a loop that 
awaits user input. Closing the window can in this simple case be done by pressing the usual cross 
sign in the top right corner.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 4.4 – Basic Tkinter window 
 
The main window is now ready to be developed even further with a variety of interface widgets, 
where different configurations of accessing these widgets can be used. This can basically be done in 
any imaginably way, but three options were considered and is shown in Figure 4.5. 
 
Figure 4.5 – Layout configurations (left, Plain window) (middle, Window with navigation buttons) (right, Window with tabs)
==PAGE:59==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
41 
 
Each of these configurations have different pros and cons for structuring the interface. The plain 
window configuration gives the user an overview of the entire program in one screen and can be very 
useful if the interface contains only a few widgets. However, the plain window setup can end up being 
somewhat confusing and unstructured if many widgets are needed with different purposes. This can 
of course be mitigated by setting a larger window size but might end up being less user-friendly since 
one might want to have multiple programs running on their computer screen simultaneously. To 
ensure a better division of calculation topics, into more manageable groups, a possibility is to use 
buttons to navigate between multiple windows. In this way, the user will be prompted with a start-up 
screen which can contain some initial widgets and general information. On this screen, navigation 
buttons will be placed which can be used to navigate between pages that contain different parameters 
needed for the calculation. This provides a quite intuitive flow since the program automatically guides 
the user through the setup and parsing of calculation parameters. The downside of this configuration 
is that it does not give an immediate overview of all the steps in the process and parameters because 
they are placed on different pages.  Furthermore, if the user wants to jump to a specific page, the user 
must go through every step by clicking “Back” and “Next” multiple times, which is a bit cumbersome 
if there are many pages.  
A solution to this could be to use the last of the three options which is the notebook setup. This setup 
is comparable with the previous one, since it also divides the parameters into multiple pages which 
instead are accessed by shuffling through named tabs in the top of the window. This gives a better 
overview of where to find or put information for a specific calculation topic e.g. the cross-section 
data or the fire type. SteFi use the notebook configuration with a fixed size window large enough to 
contain the widgets required to collect all parameters for the fire verification and creating visual 
outputs etc.  
The following pages will briefly describe the content of each tab and reasons for some of the graphical 
choices made while developing the program. A complete detailed user guide for the program can be 
found in Appendix G. 
Cross-section data:

can be chosen from a dropdown widget as seen on Figure 4.6. 
==PAGE:60==

outer rounding whereas the CHS (Circular Hollow Section) needs only outer diameter and thickness

to visualise the required inputs.  
The way Tkinter handles this, is by using the trace function on the list of profile types and call a 
definition that contains information on which entry fields and illustration to put on the interface when 
a certain profile type has been chosen in the dropdown menu. Code snippet 5 shows an example of 
how the trace function works. 
The options for the dropdown menu are created as a list in line 18. The chosen option is stored in a 
string variable (line 21). Changes to this string variable is then traced in line 24 and the definition 
“profile_change” is called which makes changes to the interface. The trace function is used for every 
dropdown widget in the program.

It is noticeable that the default value for the convective heat transfer coefficient is set to 35

severe steel heating. However, the value can, together with the values of steel and flame emissivity, 
be changed by the user.  
 
4.1.4 Calculations 
It is common practice to divide the structure of a program into two parts which is a front-end and a 
back-end. The front-end should contain all the code related to user interactions and the back-end 
should contain everything else. In the previous section, only principles and functions for user 
interaction were presented, while principles for the calculations necessary to verify or design is 
presented in this section.  
Located in the BackEnd.py file is almost all the code not related to user interaction. All code in the 
back-end is designed as definitions, which takes inputs from the front-end file and manipulates it. 
Given that all variables are defined as string variables, before a variable can be used in a calculation, 
it must be transformed to either an integer or a floating number. This is accomplished by using the 
int() or float() function as the first thing in every definition as shown in Code snippet 8.   
==PAGE:67==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
49 
 
Code snippet 8 – Conversion of strings to numbers 
1. # Area of profiles   
2. def profile_area(selectedProfile, B, H, tf, tw, r, t_RHS, r1, r2, D, t_CHS):   
3.     if selectedProfile == "I-Profile":  
4.         # Inputs converted from string to number   
5.         B = float(B); H = float(H); tf = float(tf); tw = float(tw); r = float(r) 
6.         # Calculation   
7.         a = 2*B*tf+tw*(H-2*tf)+4*r**2*(1-pi/4)   
 
Without the transformation from a string variable to a floating number or an integer, it is not possible 
to use the variable in an equation.  The value of the variable would be inserted in the equation as text 
and interpreted as text, even if the string variable only contains a number. In all definitions, the 
variables which are used in calculations are converted to doubles to ensure the definitions will be 
executed correctly if the user inserts either an integer or floating number. As seen in Code snippet 8, 
the selectedProfile variable is not converted to a double, but is used as a string variable to select the 
right formula for calculating the area, as it varies with the profile type. This principle is used in every 
definition to differentiate between calculations every time a function has multiple calculation methods 
which are dependent on the options selected by the user. 
Given that there are several places in the program where the user has multiple options to choose from, 
the definitions in the back-end should be able to run, independently on the options selected. This 
means that all definitions should be able to handle all possible combinations of the definitions 
executed prior to them. In the case with the deterioration of the material, the two options have 
different outputs with two deterioration factors found via the table in Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 
2007) or one found by the equation developed by Hertz (Hertz, 2007). This is solved by always taking 
two deterioration factors as inputs. With the definition for the deterioration factor found by Hertz’ 
equation only returning one value, that value is applied to two variables in the front-end, thus creating 
the required two variables, one for the strength reduction and one for the stiffness reduction. This 
solves the particular issue of having two outputs from the tabulated values from Eurocode and one 
from the Hertz’ equation, and the same concept has been applied elsewhere when needed. The 
definitions used for the verification process have a straight forward calculation process, which 
contradicts the definitions used for the design process. In the verification process, the load-bearing 
capacity is found for at specific temperature with all the variables known, whereas in the design 
process the critical temperature needs to be found and then the necessary insulation thickness can be 
determined as described in Section 3.4.1. Finding the critical temperature and the insulation thickness 
is done with an iterative process with two steps.  
==PAGE:68==
 
50                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
The first step in the process is finding the critical temperature. To do so, the deterioration factor that 
will cause the load-bearing capacity to be equal to the applied load should be found, but the 
deterioration factor takes the temperature as an input. This would in principle mean that we should 
give the critical temperature as the input, but since that is an unknown a different approach should be 
used. The deterioration factor is instead calculated for all temperatures from 20 °C to 1100 °C with 
an increment of 0.1 °C. This results in a list with deterioration factors which is then inserted in the 
load-bearing calculation one at a time. By iterating over the list, the deterioration factor which causes 
the load-bearing capacity to be just less than the applied load is found. The temperature is found by 
adding 0.1 °C for each iteration, which also means that the accuracy of the critical temperature will 
be within 0.1 °C. The second step is to find the insulation thickness, which is found by the same 
means as the critical temperature, but by iteration over the insulation thickness, which increases in 
increments of 0.1 mm. 
The calculation process may take around a second to complete as it is depending on the settings for 
the calculation. However, during the calculation process, two plots are generated and stored in the 
program directory, to be displayed in the program and in the report, which takes almost a full second 
on its own. The actual calculation time for the results is almost instant. Since the largest time 
consumer is generating the plots, the quality of the image could be reduced from the 300 dpi it 
currently is. The gain in calculation time for a quality of 200 dpi is, however, only around 0.1 second, 
which does not compensate for the lower quality image.   
When performing a design or verification of an element with the use of a DS Parametric fire curve, a 
calculation is made to determine the equivalent standard fire resistance time based on the maximum 
steel temperature found for that specific parametric fire. This equivalent resistance time will be 
printed in the report and exported to the BIM program as a helpful information for the user, to 
compare the effect of using compartment properties for a fully developed fire exposure with the use 
of standard fire exposure. This can also be used to investigate, whether the resistance times suggested 
by a given legislation are well calibrated in accordance with the actual properties of the compartment. 
Figure 4.13 shows an illustrative example of how the determination of the equivalent standard fire 
resistance time is conducted. By finding the maximum or critical temperature for the parametric fire 
and comparing this with the steel temperature for the standard fire exposure with the same insulation 
options, the time for when this steel temperature is reached can then be extracted from the calculation. 
 
==PAGE:69==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
51 
 
793.4 °C
Equivalent time: 
49 minuts 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
4.1.5 Generating Documentation 
The calculation module takes many inputs to perform the verification and design of the elements, as 
well as it returns many results. To save and store the input and output data, and to provide 
documentation for the calculation, the user can print a report, which contains all the information of 
the calculation. The report can only be created after a calculation has been completed and will be 
saved as a .pdf file with a user defined name at a given destination with Figure 4.14 as an example of 
a report for a column with insulation designed for a standard fire. (See Appendix E) 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 4.14 – Example of SteFi documentation report
Figure 4.13 – Determination of equivalent standard fire resistance time
==PAGE:70==
 
52                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
The report is generated with a Python module called ReportLab, which has multiple options for 
creating the document containing the report. One of the options is the pdfgen package. This package 
contains the canvas object which operates by ”painting” content onto a blank canvas. The canvas 
object can add text, pictures, lines etc. by defining the placement with xy-coordinates as seen in Code 
snippet 9, where the font size is also changed. 
Code snippet 9 – Adding text and line to canvas and changing the font size 
1. canvas.setFont("Helvetica", 20)  
2. canvas.drawString(10, 20, "SteFi Report")   
3. canvas.setFont("Helvetica", 12)   
4. canvas.drawString(10, 34, "This report contains a structural fire safety analysis
 created with SteFi")   
 
This resembles the method used to place widgets for the GUI, which was the reason for choosing the 
canvas package and ReportLab module as the method for generating the report. The default option in 
the canvas package places the (x,y) = (0,0) in the bottom left corner with the x and y increasing to the 
right and upwards respectively. The (0,0) coordinate can be moved to the top left corner (similar to 
the default for Tkinter), with x still increasing to the right but y increasing downwards, by the 
command bottomup=0. The bottomup command effectively flips the canvas around the horizontal 
symmetry line, which affects the drawImage function while the drawString and line functions are 
unaffected. If bottomup=0 all inserted images are flipped around their horizontal axis which makes 
them unreadable. Every time an image is inserted, the effect of the bottomup=0 command must be 
neutralised before the image is inserted and then activated again before the next line or string of text 
is inserted. To do so, the state of the canvas is saved, then flipped, and then the image is inserted, 
before the state of the canvas is restored to the state it was at the time the state was saved as seen in 
Code snippet 10. 
Code snippet 10 – Neutralising the bottomup=0 command when inserting images by scaling the canvas 
1. canvas.saveState()  
2. canvas.scale(1.0, -1.0)   
3. canvas.drawImage(os.getcwd()+"\dependencies\pictures\Simple_Point.png", 370+rm,  
-y + 12, width=-100, height=-100, mask="auto")   
4. canvas.restoreState()  
 
Depending on the options the user has selected for the calculation, the data in the report varies. 
Therefore, the structure of the report should be dynamic and able to adapt. Usually the xy-coordinates 
are typed in as numbers for each inserted item, but since the data in the report varies, the placement 
of each item is decided by a fixed spacing that takes the varying lines of data into account as shown 
==PAGE:71==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
53 
 
in Code snippet 10. Here the x and y-coordinate are replaced with 370+rm and -y+12 respectively, 
where in 370+rm the rm is a pre-set value of the x-coordinate for anything placed in the right column 
if two columns of text are used. The -y in -y+12 is the adaptable line spacing, which is increased for 
every item placed on the canvas, and in this case, the y-coordinate should be negative as the 
orientation of the canvas is flipped by line 2 in Code snippet 10.  
 
4.1.6 Compiling 
The development of the program has been done using a Python interpreter which allows for easy 
testing and debugging of the code while it is being written. The interpreter also helps the developer 
by checking the code and using a utility called intellisense, which auto-completes code syntax 
throughout the development in contradiction to working in for example notepad which is a raw 
formatting base with not no intelligent tools. 
However, when the code is completed and working, this is not the most user-friendly way of running 
the program. Instead, the code should be compiled so the user does not have to install interpreters to 
be able to launch the program and use it for steel verification. Compiling Python code is not as simple 
and intuitive as for C# or C++ which can be carried out directly from a Microsoft Visual Studio 
interpreter. Therefore, to compile the Python code, a special module needs to be used. Multiple 
modules can be used for this, each with different setups, utilities and pros/cons.  
The module initially used for doing the compiling was called cx_Freeze and was based on a Python 
setup file containing necessary data for the module to run a so-called build of the code. In this way, 
cx_Freeze could convert the code into either a build folder, containing the same project files as the 
development folder, or convert the project with the associated Python environment into a single 
Microsoft, Linux or Mac OS installer. This installer could then be distributed to any user who would 
be able to install it on their computer and run it as any other installed program. Installing the program 
on the computer results in faster launch times of the interface, and makes the program run smoothly. 
However, compiling to an executable installer file did not work when the program was using the 
matplotlib module for generating plots. This was a big issue since the plotting of input data, such as 
the fire curve, as well as the results of the steel heating is of great importance. Therefore, another 
compiler module called PyInstaller was considered. 
In contradiction to cx_Freeze, PyInstaller does not need a Python setup file to create a build of the 
code. PyInstaller does not create executable installer files, instead it has the possibility to convert the 
==PAGE:72==
 
54                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
code into a single executable file that, when executed, opens the program directly from the file without 
the need of installation. However, compiling to a single executable, makes the launch time of the 
program longer than the installed version but the plotting module works when using this setup, which 
ultimately ended up as the decisive factor when choosing the compiler. Tests of the launch time of 
the current version of the program was conducted to see the impact of using the single file setup. The 
program was tested on three different computers, all with 8GB’s of RAM installed but with three 
different processor cores (i5-3210M, i5-6200 and i7-4720HQ). The time passed from executing the 
program file until the interface showed, varied between 10-25 seconds depending on the computer. 
25 seconds is a fairly long time to wait for the program to launch and with no loading screen or 
indication of something happening, the user might get confused and impatient. The idea of having a 
loading screen seems to be possible with the PyInstaller module, since it allows for the attachment of 
a file to run before executing the main script. However, this is not implemented in the current version.    
Compiling the code with PyInstaller is done by running the following command in the windows 
terminal (command prompt) after the directory has been changed to the current directory of the source 
code: 
pyinstaller SteFi.py -F -w -i "path_to_icon" 
where 
SteFi.py is the name of the main script 
 
-F indicates that the code is converted to a single file 
 
-w indicates that the program will run with no console in the background 
 
-i puts a logo on the executable taken from the path given afterwards 
The program is currently compiled to work on Windows OS but can also be compiled and distributed 
to Linux and Mac users if the compiling command is changed.  
 
4.2 BIM Integration Module 
The BIM integration module has been developed in a cooperative process with a graduate student, 
Lorenzo Beltrani, working on a similar project at DTU (Beltrani, 2018)1. Beltrani’s work should 
initially focus on developing a BIM integrated verification tool for steel, concrete and wood elements 
in a non-fire situation. However, during the time of the two projects, the scope somewhat changed, 
and a more specific cooperation was carried out. Beltrani focussed on implementing the methods and 
                                                 
1 Work in progress – definitive title not settled 
==PAGE:73==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
55 
 
writing the code for the data export/import from and into the BIM environment. Collaborating with 
Beltrani has mainly been about exchanging knowledge and discussing different possibilities for data 
export/import and how this data should be structured in a data exchange file. 
So, the calculation and BIM integration modules have been treated as two separate parts given that 
the processes within each one is completely different. As mentioned in Section 2.1.2, the chosen 
method to create the integration to Autodesk Revit is by developing a Dynamo script to handle the 
export and import of data. Dynamo is an add-on program which is included in the standard installation 
of Revit but could also be used as a stand-alone platform. Dynamo provides the user with the 
possibility to do visual programming in Revit, rather than traditional textual programming. Dynamo 
comes with a standard library of components which can be extended by downloading additional 
packages from user forums or creating user defined custom components with textual programming. 
The integration module is created using both standard dynamo components and custom components 
written in Python. The components written in Python primarily have the function of utilising loops 
and if-statements which Dynamo otherwise does not support.  
Dynamo gives the user the ability to run scripts in two ways. Either as a constant flow, where the 
script is executed automatically every time any input changes, or by clicking the “Run” button for 
executing the script once, the same way traditional textual programming works. Using a continuous 
flow for the data extraction, export, and import could be problematic given that the user might not 
always be completely sure of which export and import files belongs to what element. Therefore, the 
one-click execution method is applied as it gives more control to the user of what and when to export. 
 
The export/import process is divided into three scripts, two for the data exportation (Revit and IFC) 
and one for the data importation. All three can run through the Dynamo Player, which is a feature in 
Dynamo that allows the user to run Dynamo scripts without opening Dynamo. The advantage of using 
the Dynamo Player rather than opening the Dynamo script is that it is easier to use if the user is not 
accustomed to Dynamo or visual programming in general. The Dynamo Player runs in the Revit 
SteFi
Revit model
Export
Revit model
IFC model
Import
Figure 4.15 – Process of verification or design of an element in a BIM model
==PAGE:74==
 
56                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
window rather than in a separate Dynamo window, which makes it appear more integrated and the 
inputs required by the Dynamo script is organised and presented in a foreseeable manner as seen on 
Figure 4.17. Running the script from Dynamo requires the user to know where inputs are needed in 
the script, which can be difficult to localise if the scripts are large as seen on Figure 4.16. One benefit 
of running the script through Dynamo instead of Dynamo Player, is that the user can preview the data 
processing at different steps in the process and not only the final product. 
Another benefit of using Dynamo is that it can extract data from other file types than the native Revit 
format. Dynamo can also interact with IFC files which will allow the user to use the BIM integration 
even if the user does not have a Revit license.  
 
Figure 4.16 – Dynamo script content for exporting data by selection in Revit

selection process (right) 
==PAGE:75==
