57 
 
The BIM integration module has been developed for Revit 2018 with the use of Dynamo version 
1.3.2. Using earlier or later versions of Dynamo may result in errors when running the scripts. 
Furthermore, for the scripts to be able to run, two additional Dynamo packages should be installed 
which can be done directly from the Dynamo interface. The two packages are; archi-lab.net and 
Rhythm. Additionally, custom Dynamo nodes, developed by Beltrani, should be placed in a folder 
and the path referred correctly in the Dynamo settings. 
 
4.2.1 Data Extraction 
Revit is a very complex program with many features and the capability to store a vast amount of data, 
which can make extracting data a bit tricky. The data structure of Revit can be difficult to understand 
and extracting a property value is not as easy as one would guess. Through Dynamo components, 
many different properties can be accessed and extracted, but the difficult part is to create a script that 
will get the right property every time, no matter the circumstances. 
Extracting the geometric data for a selected profile should be as simple as finding the property of the 
height, width, length etc. and selecting the value assigned to that property. The most obvious way to 
extract a property could be by searching for the name of the wanted property e.g. “Width” in a list of 
properties. This would lead to all properties with the name “Width” and their values to be selected, 
which should only be one, but this is not always the case. The user could have created a custom 
property and called it “Width”, which would be returned together with the actual width of the selected 
profile. Furthermore, if the language settings in Revit is set to anything other than English, the width 
of the profile could be named differently, and thus the real width would not be extracted. Searching 
for properties by name could then be a source of errors and is therefore not suitable as the method for 
extracting properties. Instead, the name of the property could be substituted with the symbol for the 
property, but as it turns out, the symbol of the property for the width does also change according to 
language, as it is denoted as “b” in Europe and as “bf” in America (State, 2018). In this case the width 
is instead extracted by a geometrical work-around where the element is cut with a plane which returns 
a surface containing the cross-section. This surface contains a list of points which is evaluated to 
extract all the geometrical data, including the width. 
One of the challenges with data extraction from BIM-models is that models contain varying amounts 
of data as well as different data. Depending on the intended use of a given model, the kind of data 
that is stored varies, which leads to multiple possible scenarios when searching for a specific property 
==PAGE:76==
 
58                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
in different BIM-models. A possible scenario could be that the property is a custom property which 
has not been created in a given model, or just for a specific element in that model. This would make 
it impossible to find the property and thus extract its value, or the property could have been created 
for the given element, but not have a stored value.   
Models can contain the same amount and kind of data, but it can be structured differently which 
affects the method for extracting the data. If a simple program should be able to find and extract the 
value of a property, the location of the property needs to be the same for each element, on which the 
extraction method should be applied. This places a major responsibility on the people creating the 
model, since the exported values may be wrong or missing if the user have assigned properties to an 
instance of an element or to a family/type. 
The amount of data stored and accessible in a Revit file is considerably more extensive than an IFC 
file, and thus the method for extracting the data is different. One of the major differences is that only 
the geometric data of a column or beam is stored in the IFC model, which considerable reduces the 
amount of data which can be extracted. Furthermore, Revit gives the opportunity to store data 
regarding the boundary conditions and loads, but this information is usually stored in reports or FEM 
models and may therefore not be available for extraction in the Revit model. Even if the user has 
added the information to the model, it cannot be taken into consideration. The boundary conditions 
are "fixed" by default and it is not possible to extract the information from a single element, whether 
it is fixed to the ground or to another element (framed). Loads are usually added as an "area load" 
and since the stiffness’s of all elements are not known, it is not accurate to divide the load by the 
number of elements. 
 
4.2.2 Data Export 
Once the desired data is extracted from the model, it needs to be structured in a certain way, so the 
properties always appear in the same sequence when exported. This is important as the calculation 
module reads the exported data based on a predefined sequence. The export file could in principle 
only contain the values of the properties, but to increase the readability of the content of the export 
file, when the export file is opened, the name of the property and the unit is exported as well. The 
data is exported as a matrix with the property names in column one, their values in column two, and 
the units in column three, with each property in its own row. As for now, the two file formats available 
to export the data in are as mentioned in Section 2.1.2 the .xlsx and .csv format. In case the export 
==PAGE:77==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
59 
 
file is in the .xlsx format the matrix adopts the table structure of Excel and organises the data 
accordingly, and for the .csv format, each property has its own line with the name, value, and unit 
separated by a comma.  
Depending on whether the model is a Revit model or an IFC model has an influence on the code that 
structures the data when exported. With IFC models, the element properties are always structured in 
the same order when extracted which makes it easy to rearrange the properties in the desired order. 
With Revit models, the order of the element properties is not always in the same way, which makes 
the rearranging more complicated and requires a different method than for the IFC model. This issue 
is especially important regarding the identification of the cross-section type, which is a property that 
is stored in the element in the IFC model but not in the Revit model. When the IFC file is created, an 
algorithm, developed by Autodesk, evaluates the lines and the arcs contained in the perimeter of the 
section to define if a shape can be represented by a parametric form. However, not all the cross-
sections are recognised and will in that case be exported as an "arbitrary profile". To solve this issue, 
an algorithm was developed both for defining the cross-section for the elements extracted from the 
Revit model, and for the arbitrary profiles obtained from the IFC model. The algorithm is based on 
the lines and arcs as well and identifies the profile type and adds it to the export file. 
The same method is used when the data is exported from the calculation module and added to the 
original exchange file from the BIM model. In this case the export values are appended to the end of 
the original export file, instead of creating a new separate file. This means that all the exported data 
for a given element are placed in the same file, which eases the process of quality assuring the data 
exported between the two modules. The data from the calculation is appended differently depending 
on the export file format. For .csv files, the calculated data is directly appended to the end of the 
original export file but for the .xlsx files, a specific row and column is dedicated to each exported 
property.  
 
4.2.3 Data Import 
The process of importing data resembles the process of exporting data to some degree, given that the 
structure of the data is determined by the export process. Compared to the data import process for the 
calculation module, the import process for the integration module is a bit more complex. In both cases 
the data in the export file is accessed through a predetermined path defined by the export structure. 
Unlike in the calculation module, where only the value of the property is assigned to a variable, in 
==PAGE:78==
 
60                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
the integration module, there are no variable or property to assign the value to, except from a property, 
already inside the Revit model, called “Fire Rating”. The property is intended for a fire rating of the 
element, but unless additional information is added, the rating itself is useless. Without information 
of the load, boundary conditions, and insulation, the fire rating is not adequate. Therefore, when the 
fire data for an element is imported, 11 or 14, depending on whether the element is a beam or column, 
new properties are created and assigned to an element instance. This instance is targeted inside the 
Revit model by the element ID placed in the first row of the .xlsx file or .csv file created in the 
exportation phase. The names of the new properties are imported from the output file from SteFi by 
using the data from the first column in the matrix, the values from the second and units from the third. 
Figure 4.18 shows the property set for a selected beam before and after data has been imported from 
a SteFi calculation. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 4.18 – Property set for a selected beam before (top) and after (bottom) a 
SteFi calculation has been completed and imported in Revit 
==PAGE:79==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
61 
 
The import process in both modules is designed to overwrite existing data if values are already stored 
for a given property. For the import in the integration module, this process ensures that only one fire 
resistance time, loads, etc. is assigned to an element. Otherwise, if data were imported multiple times 
for the same element, and multiple properties were named the same, it would be impossible to identify 
the right one. Currently, import of data back into the BIM model is only possible for a Revit model, 
given that integration to Revit has been the focus of Beltrani’s project. 
4.3 Summary 
Dividing the BIM-integrated design process into two modules, each with their own purpose and 
process, has allowed them to be developed to suit that specific purpose regardless of the other module. 
The two modules are constructed very differently and face different challenges, given that they 
perform two completely different tasks and operate in different environments. However, to achieve a 
good exchange of data between the modules, the individual parts involving data exchange needs to 
be developed in cooperation where special attention has been given to the structure, amount and what 
data to place in the exchange file. 
Developing the calculation module, SteFi, with the Tkinter GUI package has allowed for an interface 
which provide the opportunity to use the program as a stand-alone software or in cooperation with 
the BIM integration module. SteFi is structured as a notebook, which means that there are tabs for 
navigating through the different pages containing different inputs needed for the verification and 
design of elements. Almost every input required for the calculation is a variable that can be defined 
by the user except the axes on the plots. Limiting the variety of scenarios have been necessary and 
comprises the amount of boundary conditions and load combinations, profile types, and fire types 
available to choose from. SteFi works as a stand-alone program compiled to an executable file that 
does not require an interpreter for it to run. Currently it is compiled to work only on Windows 
operation systems, but could be compiled to work on other operating systems as well. With the 
calculation executed, SteFi provides the possibility for plotting steel heating curves and printing a 
report to provide documentation of the inputs used to perform the calculation. Furthermore, it enables 
the user to export the results of the calculation so that it can be imported to the BIM model. 
The integration module is developed as Dynamo scripts which can run through the Dynamo Player 
and can be divided into three processes. The three processes are extracting data, exporting data, and 
importing data from the calculation module. Extracting and exporting data is conducted in one script, 
which can either extract data from an IFC file or a Revit model and, depending on the choice, export 
==PAGE:80==
 
62                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
various amounts of data. The third process of importing the data from the calculation module is 
conducted in its own script, which creates new properties to contain the results of the calculation. The 
integration module has been developed in close collaboration with Lorenzo Beltrani, a graduate 
student at DTU, who has been responsible for implementing all code in the module. 
Together, the two modules are considered as an integrated and automatic fire design for steel 
elements. The developed software has the potential to not only save time, by automating the design 
and verification of the elements, but also enhance the usability of the BIM model, by adding structural 
fire safety information. At the current state, the data extracted and used in the calculation is limited 
to geometrical data for the element.  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:81==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
63 
 
5 Case Analysis 
 
This chapter contains the use of SteFi and tests of its various calculation setup methods on an existing 
steel structure. Testing the program on an existing structure highlights the possibilities of working 
with such a verification tool, and how it can be used in real time to support the design and verification 
process of structural fire safety. With various calculation settings, the program provides the possibility 
to compare different combinations of verification method, deterioration factors and fire types to 
clarify the outcome of choosing a certain procedure.  
The structure investigated in this case study is an open car-park built in France, previously analysed 
in the work conducted by Iwona Budny (Budny, 2010). Budny’s thesis, among other things, compares 
the verification methods of different national codes together with the methods given in Eurocode. In 
brief, Budny concludes that for the case of unprotected exposure of the structure, a 60 minutes 
resistance time for the standard fire curve results in more conservative values than either of the Danish 
and Swedish method, both using fully developed fires. However, if the structure is protected by an 
insulation material, the amount of material needed to prove the resistance of the elements is a lot 
smaller for the parametric fires than for the R60 standard fire. This indicates that, from an economical 
perspective, it is better to use the parametric fires since they result in a structure that can hold the 
required design loads for the full duration of the fire (including cooling phase). It is therefore 
appropriate to test and use SteFi for a comparison of the verification and design with both standard 
fire exposure and parametric fire exposure. 
 
5.1 Description of structure 
The car-park is built with two levels for parking (ground and first floor) with the following 
dimensions; 15 metres wide, 32 metres long and 3 metres in height from the ground to the first floor. 
The resulting compartment area is therefore 480 m², capable of containing 24 cars on each level. The 
structure consists of steel columns and steel beams of different sizes. The floor, resting on the steel 
beams, is made as a composite slab consisting of Cofrastra 40 steel sheets with a 120mm concrete 
slab on top. (ArcelorMittal, 2018) 
The beams are categorised as either primary beams or secondary beams, where the primary beams 
are made as IPE550 and the secondary beams are IPE400 and IPE500. All beam connections are 
==PAGE:82==
 
64                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
hinged and are therefore calculated as simply supported with either point load of uniform distributed 
load from the floor slab. The columns in the centre of the structure are made as HEB200, whereas the 
ones placed at the sides are HEA180. All columns are pinned to the ground and are free to expand. 
A total of four columns and four beams, representing all possible variations, are chosen for the 
calculation to get a reasonable sample size for a comparison of the methods. The choice of elements 
and naming convention is unified with the ones chosen by Budny to uniformize the two projects. 
Figure 5.1 illustrates the structure with the corresponding naming convention for the chosen elements. 
The structure has been modelled in Revit 2018 to test the data exchange with cross-section data 
exported for each of the eight elements. These files are used as input in SteFi where multiple analyses 
are conducted. 
 
Figure 5.1 – Naming convention of beams and columns chosen for the calculations (Budny, 2010) 
 
Figure 5.2 - Plan view of carpark structure with dimensions in mm 
 
 
==PAGE:83==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
65 
 
5.2 Loads 
The car-park is subjected to different loads which are considered in a load combination for accidental 
limit state (ALS) to estimate the fire design load. The loads considered are; the permanent self-weight 
of the concrete slab, imposed load by traffic and parking and snow load.

multiplied with a reduction factor (²/,') equal to zero as seen in Table 5.2.

beams will support the self-weight of the composite deck elements and the variable live load imposed 
==PAGE:84==
 
66                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
by traffic. This results in a load per meter beam that can be converted to a total load distributed as 
point loads to either side where the primary beams are supported by a secondary beam or a column. 
If a primary beam is resting on a secondary beam (at mid span), the point load from the primary beam 
should, in theory, be added to the uniform self-weight load of the secondary beam. However, since 
the program is not developed to consider supposition of load cases, the self-weight of the secondary 
beams will be converted to a point load in the middle by multiplying the uniform distributed self-
weight with the length of the beam. Finally, the accumulated load on the secondary beams will be 
distributed to either side where they are mounted on the columns. The calculations for the design 
loads in case of fire can be seen in Appendix D. The lengths of the beams are shorter than the ones 
stated by Budny since the lengths are exported from Revit as the actual length and not the theoretical.

5.3 Compartment Properties 
The car-park is considered as one large fire compartment with an area of 480 m² with no dividing 
wall structures. The four sides of the car-park are open and are therefore not considered as openings 
for the calculation of the opening factor, and not included when determining the thermal inertia of 
the enclosing compartment surface. Additionally, no horizontal openings are present in the slab, 
which allows for the use of the opening factor formula which only includes the area of vertical 
openings. Budny determines the actual opening factor of the compartment as: 
 
O = 
¦§⋅{?¨©
¦ª
= 0.39 ¥½
 
(5.1)
==PAGE:85==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
67 
 
However, as mentioned by Budny, the Danish regulations state a lower and upper limit for the opening 
factor: 0.01 ≤ O ≤ 0.30 ¥½, which limits the actual opening factor of 0.39 ¥½ to 0.30 ¥½. 
The thermal inertia of the compartment enclosure is relevant for the fire development as this is a 
parameter that describes the amount of heat absorbed by the compartment surfaces. Since the sides 
of the car-park are open, the only surface to absorb heat is the composite floor slab, as the ground 
floor is neglected since the heat from the fire primarily will move upwards. Budny initially calculates 
the thermal inertia based on Eurocode (DS/EN 1991-1-2, 2007) with clauses from the Danish National 
Annex (EN 1991-1-2 DK NA, 2007), but the result ends up being very high and unrealistic. Therefore, 
the approach of the Eurocode is instead used as the actual thermal inertia considered for the parametric 
fire exposure where the thermal inertia is calculated as b = 1478 ¬/¥<-½B (Budny, 2010). 
The last parameter for determining the actual fire development in the compartment is the fire load 
density (or fuel load) based on the amount of burning material considered in the car-park. In this case, 
only cars will be on fire which will have a given heat release depending on their size/category. In 
Denmark, the suggested value to use as fire load density for car-parks is 300 N¬/¥< which 
corresponds with the value found by Budny in a critical scenario where 24 cars of the highest category 
are on fire. The three parameters used for the DS Parametric fire are summed below and results in the 
fire curve shown on Figure 5.2: 
• Opening factor:

5.4 Program Validation 
Validating a program is a comprehensive process which requires numerous tests. Given that the 
current version of SteFi is the first version, it is expected that there will be some bugs and errors. 
However, it is important that the results of the calculations are accurate and validated. To validate the 
results of the calculations, a comparison with Budny’s results have been conducted. The lengths and 
loads used by Budny, listed in table Table 5.4, are used as input for the validation, with the strength 
of the steel as 235 MPa and Young’s modulus as 210 GPa. The type of insulation used for the 
comparison is a mineral fibre spray, like the one used by Budny. This spray is applied to all elements 
as a contour encasement and is stated to have a constant thermal conductivity value of; v = 0.120 
W/(m°C) (Budny, 2010). SteFi is developed to take varying conductivity values for the insulation 
depending on the temperature, so this constant value is simply used for all temperature intervals from 
0-1000 °C.

of the results from SteFi is within an acceptable margin for the current state of the program.

5.5 Design of Insulation for Car-park Elements 
The calculation settings given by SteFi can, in principle, be combined with each other in any way to 
cover a specific fire scenario. For this case study, six configurations of the calculation have been 
defined as seen in Table 5.8. The different settings for each configuration are displayed in the table 
columns where the one furthest to the right notes the verification method for either beams or columns 
(section or element analysis and temperature dependent or independent imperfection for beams and 
columns respectively). Settings not mentioned in Table 5.8 are identical for all configurations. 
SteFi generated reports for each element for each configuration can be found in Appendix E.

A total of four comparisons for the different configurations have been conducted to investigate the 
differences. The measuring unit used for the comparison is the calculated insulation thickness needed 
to support the load applied to the element if it is insulated with gypsum boards. The required insulation 
thickness reflects the load-bearing capacity of an element and will give a sufficient indication on how 
a given configuration performs.  
Gypsum boards come in standard thicknesses, so a comparison is also conducted for the thickness 
which the insulation will have when standard gypsum board thicknesses are used. The thicknesses of 
the standard gypsum boards are displayed in italic next to the minimum required insulation thickness 
and is either 6.5 mm, 9.5 mm, 12.5 mm, or 15.4 mm (Gyproc, 2018). The gypsum boards used for 
insulation are of the type Gyproc with thermal conductivity values varying with temperature, taken 
from the Swedish literature (Pettersson, et al., 1976) instead of a constant value of 0.25 W/(m °C) as 
otherwise stated in the product catalogue. It is assumed that multiple layers of gypsum board can be 
applied in any combination to create a thickness of 13 mm (2 ∙ 6.5 mm), 16 mm (6.5 mm + 9.5 mm), 
==PAGE:90==
 
72                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
19 mm (2 ∙ 9.5 mm), and 19.5 mm (3 ∙ 6.5 mm). The four different thicknesses listed (and their 
combination options) are based on regular gypsum boards from Gyproc with the fire related property; 
Euroclass A2-S1, d0. These boards are not marketed as fire protection boards but will give more 
precise result of the actual needed amount of insulation in kilograms. However, Gyproc produces a 
series of Fireboards, which are meant for fire protection of steel structures, and are provided in 
thicknesses of 15, 20 and 25 mm. These values have also been used when determining the required 
amount of insulation, to see the effect of choosing a fire protective gypsum board. The overall amount 
of insulation needed for these Fireboards are compared to the other results. Calculations for the 
required amount of insulation can be seen in Appendix F. 
The four comparisons considered are: 
• Configuration 1+2:  
Impact of fire type 
• Configuration 1+3+4: 
Impact of deterioration factor 
• Configuration 1+5:  
Impact of verification method 
• Configuration 1+6:  
Combined impact of DS Parametric fire and Hertz 
                                                     deterioration 0.2 % 
The following sections contains the results for each of the comparisons together with brief 
elaborations on the findings. The results will be discussed later, in Chapter 6.

Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements

5.5.1 Impact of Fire Type 
Configuration 1+2 
The first comparison conducted is the effect of the fire type which concerns the two fire types 
previously mentioned; ISO834 Standard fire curve and DS Parametric fire curve. When designing 
insulation with the standard fire curve, a specific resistance time is required. Budny states that no 
requirements are given for a fire resistance class of car-parks in Denmark (Budny, 2010). This is not 
entirely wrong, as the Danish Building Regulations does not state anything for car-parks specifically. 
However, from 2012 and onwards, the building regulation refers to a document containing building 
samples with prescriptive solutions (Trafik- og Byggestyrelsen, 2012). Here the resistance class for 
load-bearing elements should be chosen based on the usage category of the building and on the height 
from the ground to the floor on the top level. The prescriptive resistance classes of load-bearing 
elements for a structure with the floor on the top level, at maximum five meters above ground, is 
considered similar to the case of the car-park, because the composite slab is made for parking (with 
an acting live load) and not as a roof structure. The fire resistance class used for the beams and 
columns of the carpark is therefore set to R60 which results in a maximum gas temperature in the 
compartment of 945 °C as seen in Figure 5.3.  
 
Figure 5.3 – ISO834 Standard fire curve with the gas temperature for the R60 resistance class  
The calculation for both fire types, with the given cross-section data from the model and the 
previously described loads and insulation material, results in a required insulation thickness for each 
element as seen in Table 5.9.

5.5.2 Impact of Deterioration Factor 
Configuration 1+3+4 
The method for determining the deterioration factors is presumed to influence the amount of 
insulation needed, based on the findings in Section 3.3. A noticeable difference in the strength and 
stiffness reduction were found between the Eurocode deterioration factors for 2.0 % stress (DS/EN 
1993-1-2, 2007) and the ones found analytically with the formula developed by Hertz for the 0.2 % 
stress (Hertz, 2007). This comparison of the required insulation also includes the amount required if 
the deterioration factor for 2.0 % stress from Hertz is used. This has been done to see the impact that 
the difference in stress type has on the result. The results can be seen in Table 5.10 and on Figure 5.5.

6 Discussion 
 
In this chapter, the work and findings in the previous chapters will be discussed, with the intent to 
provide insight of the challenges and choices that were faced in the attempt to integrate structural fire 
design with a BIM oriented work process. The following section will elaborate on the findings in 
theory and how this affects the choices that needs to be considered when verifying or designing steel 
elements. A discussion of the software development process and the outcome will follow, which 
resulted in the calculation module SteFi and its integration to Revit. Lastly, the presented results of 
the case analysis will be discussed to approach a conclusion on the investigation of the currently used 
resistance class design method. 
 
6.1 Discussion of Applied Theory 
Verifying steel elements in accidental limit state for fire exposure is, on the bottom line, a simple and 
linear procedure for both beams and columns when using simple calculation methods. These 
procedures are clearly described in the regulative documents (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) and (EN 1991-
1-2 DK NA, 2007) where the use of the ISO834 Standard fire curve is the commonly used type of 
fire exposure. However, since the regulations also allow the use of other fire models, choices need to 
be made on whether to use more advanced calculation methods and include fire types such as the 
parametric fire curves (natural fire models).  
The advanced calculation methods presented in this thesis is, for beams, the limit load analysis where 
the beams are analysed on element level and for columns the inclusion of the effect of thermal 
expansion when the element is exposed to elevated temperatures. Both calculations require a better 
understanding and knowledge of theory. Especially the inclusion of thermal expansion in the 
calculations have shown to be a hurdle to overcome whilst being a hot topic in the world of structural 
fire safety. The theory presented, to account for indirect stresses occurring due to hindered thermal 
expansion, has been compared to known data, and resulted in large, unacceptable differences. 
Therefore, calculations with inclusion of hindered expansion is not included in the current version of 
SteFi. The difference found in the comparison have been difficult to explain, but as previously 
mentioned, multiple factors such as the use of 0.5 % proof stress, the use of secant modulus and no 
==PAGE:100==
 
82                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
information regarding which thermal expansion coefficient have been used, may be of great 
importance, and is definitely something that should be investigated in the future. 
Additionally, the reduction of material strength and the element expansion turns out to be a very 
important factor and is possibly one of the reasons for observed structural collapses in real buildings. 
This thesis has been situated around the effect of thermal expansion for columns only. However, the 
same effects occur in beams when they are heated, resulting in longitudinal elongation which effects 
the surrounding elements. In reality, thermal expansion should be considered for both types of 
structural elements on a system level to understand the full effect of the fire exposure. 
When determining the steel temperature of a cross-section, as a result of elevated temperatures in a 
compartment, different parameters can affect the result. The literature investigated in this thesis 
provides different methods for calculating the steel temperature over time, that in the end are based 
on the same initial heat balance equations. What differs between the method described in Eurocode 
(DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) and in the Swedish literature (Pettersson, et al., 1976) is basically the choice 
of parameters such as the steel and flame emissivity, heat transfer coefficients and configuration 
factors for shadow effects. The different parameters affect the steel heating differently and are 
important to consider. An example is the configuration factor for shadow effect from nearby elements. 
Adjusting this parameter according to the placement of elements next to each other is very important 
since the elements will be heated less if they are placed with closer distance. The configuration factor 
for shadow effects can, to some extent, be compared to the effect of the section factor because they 
both describe the amount of cross-section visible for exposure. 
Another important factor to mention is the different material deterioration methods presented. Three 
different ways of determining the reduced strength and stiffness of the steel elements have been 
investigated to understand the differences and the effect of choosing one over another. The 
deterioration factors used in the Swedish literature (Pettersson, et al., 1976) are presented in graphs 
which would result in less accurate calculations because the values would have to be read manually 
for different temperatures. Instead, using either tabulated deterioration factors from Eurocode (DS/EN 
1993-1-2, 2007) or the analytical expression from Hertz (Hertz, 2007) seems to be a better choice and 
will result in more accurate calculations. Choosing between these two methods have shown to affect 
the overall calculation quite substantial as also reflected in the results of the case analysis. Given that 
Eurocode uses 2.0 % stress for the calculation, a large difference is seen when comparing with the 
0.2 % stress suggested by Hertz as shown in Figure 3.4 in Section 3.3. 
==PAGE:101==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
83 
 
Finally, an investigation of the effect of imperfections for columns have been conducted to highlight 
the differences in literature. Eurocode and the Swedish literature suggests two different equations for 
determination of the buckling stress, both based on the initial theory of equating external and internal 
moment for the column. By deriving the buckling equation, values for the imperfection part of the 
equation have been compared to the ones from the investigated literature. The findings indicate that 
Eurocode uses a temperature dependent imperfection whilst the Swedes provides an equation with a 
temperature independent imperfection. Working with the temperature dependent imperfection would 
reflect the actual situation of elevated temperatures and probably result in more accurate calculations. 
On the other hand, using the temperature independent imperfection does give a more simplistic 
equation which is also easier implemented in the Python code. Furthermore, the results from the case 
analysis indicates that a reduced amount of insulation is needed when the temperature independent 
imperfection is used which is interesting for its application when taking an economic perspective.  
    
6.2 Discussion of Software Development 
One of the initial objectives for this project was:  
“Implement structural fire design in a BIM environment through software development” 
This specific objective contains a large solution frame with many possible outcomes. The approach 
has been to develop a calculation software that runs independent on the choice of BIM platform, 
resulting in a generic piece of software that can adapt to whatever platform a user may prefer. The 
developed calculation software, SteFi, contains the necessary input and options to perform structural 
fire design of steel elements, and does give the option to run both verification and design calculations 
based on manual inserted data if no BIM model is available. The implementation part of the objective 
has been realised by choosing a specific BIM platform to showcase the integration with SteFi. 
Autodesk Revit has been used for this purpose as it is the most commonly used platform for BIM in 
Denmark (Gjelsted, 2017). Revit models are used throughout different phases of a construction 
project both by the project owner(s), architects, and contractors, which results in large amounts of 
data stored in specific models. This data can be used for multiple purposes, including the design and 
verification of the structure when exposed to fire. By creating a link between the model and 
calculation procedures within the different fields in the construction industry, the model could play 
an even more important role in the future, where most, if not all, design work could be based on the 
model. 
==PAGE:102==
 
84                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
Revit models will often be at a level of detail where structural elements have been designed and 
placed, and information regarding size, materials and surface treatments of rooms are present. These 
kind of data are required when a performance-based design approach is used and is therefore the focal 
point when extracting data from a model. SteFi is currently developed to import cross-section data 
and length of a chosen load-bearing element. This limit is not determined by the possibilities for 
import in SteFi, but limited by the data available for extraction in the model. Information regarding 
loads and boundary conditions are one of the shortcomings in the Revit environment. Although Revit 
does provide options for applying loads and choosing end fixations for elements, it certainly is not a 
Finite Element Program (FEM). Other programs provide better solutions for structural analysis where 
loads and boundary conditions are handled well and used to calculate the structural response. One 
would think that an integration to a FEM program would be a better choice because of the importance 
of accurate boundary conditions and structural response. This may be true; however, FEM models 
are often only created as statically detailed models and does not contain information about the 
environment in which an element is placed. This works out fine when doing normal ultimate limit 
state or service limit state verification, but for fire design, more data is needed, at least when 
considering natural fire models.  
The use of parametric fires has especially been an interesting part of the project and has been a topic 
discussed throughout the project period. Extracting parametric fire data from a model was initially 
set as a priority since this would dramatically improve the potential of such a BIM integration. If 
information regarding the compartment geometry (size and openings) could be extracted from a 
model, an accurate determination of the opening factor could be provided for the parametric fire 
curve. Furthermore, if walls and slabs would be assigned with a composition of materials, the exact 
thermal inertia of the compartment could be calculated. Lastly, if the model would be at a level of 
detail where the interior was furnished, this information could be used to determine the actual fuel 
load inside a given compartment. Altogether, this information would be able to reflect an actual fire 
development. In the end, gathering compartment data is very dependent on the quality and detail level 
of the model which entails great responsibility to the user. If the chosen detail level for a project is 
low, such data is probably not available and would cause a problem if the fire verification relies on 
it. On the bottom line, SteFi does not import compartment property data from the model since this is 
not exported in the Dynamo process developed in cooperation with Lorenzo Beltrani. However, 
developing a method for this extraction should certainly be considered in a future project to enhance 
the BIM integration and increase the potential of the developed software. 
==PAGE:103==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
85 
 
Since SteFi runs as a stand-alone software, certain requirements need to be met in terms of the 
calculation flow process and data exchange. The data exchange is handled using a specified file 
format, either .xlsx or .csv, which contains the data exported from the BIM model. Other file formats 
were considered and could be implemented in the program since it only relies on getting specific data 
placed in a specific order. The order of which data is written to and from the exchange file has been 
of great importance in the development of the software. The program currently imports data bound 
to specific rows (cells in Excel) which makes the data import less adaptable to varying data structures. 
This process could possibly be made in a more ingenious way for example by writing an algorithm 
that analyses the data file and automatically searches for required parameter values. The process for 
exporting results from SteFi is handled in a less fixed manner, where data will be written to the file 
based on the amount of data already stored in the file. In this way, result parameters will always be 
written to the first empty row, ensuring that no data will be overwritten, and it automatically adapts 
to the length of the file which may vary depending on how the element data was exported originally.  
When SteFi has imported the data, and missing input values have been filled, calculations can be 
initiated with various options. This is where the second project objective comes into play: 
“Ensure fast and accurate calculations” 
Awaiting results from a calculation is usually annoying and may end up making the software less 
likely to be used. Therefore, ensuring a fast calculation procedure is of great importance as well as 
trusting the results to be accurate. SteFi calculations have been tested to be executed in approximately 
one second when plot images are generated and saved to the working directory, and approximately 
0.1 second for raw result generation. This short calculation time is acceptable and is in coherence 
with the initial goal. It has also been shown, that the program does calculate quite accurate based on 
the comparison with the results generated by Budny (Budny, 2010). However, since the program has 
not been verified by an external source, its validity is still to be questioned and should undergo 
thorough investigation and testing in the future. Although SteFi runs quite fast calculations, the actual 
process of running fire design in a BIM environment with an external software should be an effortless 
task. Therefore, it has been prioritized to make the interaction with both the Dynamo export module 
and SteFi as easy as possible. This has been achieved by simplifying the needed inputs as much as 
possible for the export of data in Dynamo Player, so the user will be presented with a clear overview. 
The same line of thoughts has been applied to SteFi, where the interface has been developed in a 
sensible and tangible way to ease the use of the program. 
==PAGE:104==
 
86                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
An effort has also been made to present the results of the calculations in a readable way within the 
program. Results are basically shown in their own tab together with settings for the calculations. 
However, this could be organized differently for example by creating a pop-up result window or even 
just a blank tab on the interface where the results would show solely. The amount of results shown 
has also been up for discussion since a lot of the information is important for the overall understanding 
of the results. For this reason, creating a report containing all necessary input and output data was the 
chosen solution. In this way, only important results are displayed directly in the program interface for 
the user to decide on whether to run a different analysis or accept the data presented. Another part of 
displaying results is the export of data from SteFi back into the BIM model. Deciding on which result 
parameters to send back to the model is a difficult task. Send to little data - the results will not make 
sense and will end up being redundant. Send too much data – the model will be flooded with data and 
the user cannot distinguish between relevant and redundant data. For now, data such as the fire design 
load applied to the element, resulting load-bearing capacity, fire type and properties, resistance time 
and insulation details are send to the model. However, information about the boundary conditions is 
currently not exchanged, which is not ideal for the overall overview of the results. The reason for this 
is, as previously described, that Revit does not handle boundary conditions and loads well, so this 
should be investigated together with improvements for the boundary settings in SteFi. If the user 
would like to see the detail on the boundary conditions for the calculated element, they should instead 
refer to the generated report. 
Finally, when the results of the calculations are imported into the BIM model, the different result 
parameters are created for all elements of that element type but only the selected element (instance) 
will receive the values. These values can now be accessed for that particular element and used for 
other purposes if needed. If the user chooses to modify the model by for example changing the 
element types, their lengths or changes are made to the loads that are applied to the elements, the 
result values generated by SteFi might not be valid anymore and should therefore be checked and 
possibly re-calculated. 
 
6.3 Discussion of Results 
To ensure that SteFi could be used to investigate the impact of the differences found in Chapter 3, 
and the results to be credible, the calculations performed in the program had to be validated against 
results from an independent source. This was possible by choosing the case study of the car-park 
==PAGE:105==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
87 
 
where a data set (Budny, 2010) were available to compare the results from SteFi against. Comparing 
results for different steps in the calculation exposes any differences in the source code for SteFi and 
the calculation procedure used to generate the data set that was compared with. As seen in Section 
5.4, the results from SteFi differs from the compared data set with a margin of difference which 
increases from the steel temperature, to the critical temperature, and to the insulation thickness as 
shown in Table 6.1. 
Table 6.1 – Average difference in results for validation 
 
Steel temperature 
 
 
 
ISO834 
DS Parametric 
Critical temperature 
Insulation thickness 
Difference 
0.26 % 
0.78 % 
1.65 % 
5.36 % 
 
As mentioned in Section 5.4, the differences in steel temperature could be caused by the difference 
in the calculation of the perimeter. This would also affect the critical temperature as the perimeter is 
used in the calculation of the critical temperature. Without the calculation procedure clearly stated 
for the calculation of the critical temperatures in the data set that is used for comparison, it is 
impossible to know if that is the only reason for the difference in the critical temperature. 
Furthermore, it is seen in Table 5.6 that the critical temperatures for Beam A and B differs more than 
what is acceptable, but since results for the other elements are comparable, the difference for Beam 
A and B is assumed to be an error in the data set.  
The differences in critical temperature is reflected in the calculated insulation thickness where the 
results for Beam A and B again differs more than for the other elements. Interestingly, the required 
insulation thickness differs more for Column 2 than for the other elements, except Beam A and B, 
despite the difference in the critical temperature is similar for all elements. If the elements with 
possible erroneous calculations, Beam A and B, are neglected in the comparison, the total difference 
in insulation thickness is within an acceptable margin.  
The critical temperatures for these elements are fairly high, which indicates that the utilisation of the 
elements is relatively low at ultimate limit state. A high critical temperature means that only a thin 
layer of insulation is required. When the different configurations are compared it is based on the 
insulation thickness, but it could as well have been the critical temperature, since the calculation of 
the insulation thickness is based on the critical temperature. The insulation thickness varies depending 
on the insulation material, but since the same material and calculation of the thickness is used for 
==PAGE:106==
 
88                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
every configuration, the insulation thickness can be used for the comparison. Comparing the 
insulation thickness instead of the critical temperature presents the result in a more tangible way, than 
if the temperatures were compared. 
Comparing insulation rather than the critical temperature allows for the differences between the 
configurations to be presented as a total weight of the insulation. The required thicknesses can be 
compared and converted to standard thicknesses of the insulation material which provides a 
perspective to a real-life application. When the thickness is converted to standard dimensions, the 
difference between configurations changes depending on whether the required thicknesses are close 
to an actual thickness and if it is lower or higher. Comparing the total weight of the insulation for the 
actual thicknesses does return a slightly different result than for the calculated required thicknesses, 
which is both less accurate but also more correct. When comparing the impact of the different settings 
for the configurations, it is less accurate to use the weight of the converted thicknesses. The weight 
of the converted thicknesses is sensitive to the intervals of the standard dimensions, as every 
calculated thickness is rounded up, given that it is a minimum thickness. If all the thicknesses for one 
setting were just above a standard thickness, the difference between the configurations would be 
greatly influenced by the intervals for the standard dimensions, as seen for the Fireboard comparisons. 
Therefore, the total weight of the insulation is both listed for the required and actual thicknesses of 
the insulation. 
The results for the required thicknesses, as seen in Table 6.2 reveals that the calculation procedure 
listed in Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) differ from the alternatives presented in Chapter 3. 
Table 6.2 – Summed weight of insulation for all configurations 
 
Configuration  
 
1 
2 
3 
4 
5 
6 
Weight of insulation 
555.3 _µ
232.9 _µ 
552.1 _µ 779.5 _µ 
538.9 _µ 328.4 _µ 
 
The analysis of the impact of the fire type shows a significant difference in the amount of insulation 
required, with the design for the DS Parametric fire requiring much less insulation than the design for 
the ISO834 Standard fire. The parametric fire for this car-park has a quick temperature development 
and a relatively short duration as seen on Figure 5.2. This means that the steel is heated quickly but 
not for long, and thus the insulation required is less than for a 60-minute ISO834 Standard fire. If the 
required resistance time was reduced, the shorter duration of the ISO834 Standard fire would lead to 
==PAGE:107==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
89 
 
a lower steel temperature and insulation thickness. Then, the difference would be less, and in some 
cases, dependent on the resistance time, the DS Parametric fire could result in a thicker insulation 
layer than the ISO834 Standard fire. In this case however, it shows that there is a significant economic 
advantage of using the DS Parametric fire, given that the amount of insulation can be reduced with 
58.1 %. 
As expected, performing an element analysis showed no difference in the required insulation 
compared to the sectional analysis, given that the beams in the structure are all simply supported. 
Beams with fixed connections would be able to support a larger load, and thus not require as much 
insulation, if the load-bearing capacity is determined by an element analysis compared to a section 
analysis. Fixed-end beams are rarely seen in reality, because it requires the beam to be mounted to an 
element with relative stiffness equal to that of ground (bedrock) preventing any form of rotation at 
the connection. This is not the case if a steel beam is mounted on steel columns as a part of a frame. 
Only in situations where a beam is located in one of the first floors of a high-rise building, could it 
be considered as fixed, as the columns have considerably larger cross-sections and are much stiffer 
than the beam. Otherwise, the beam and columns would rotate relative to each other at the connection 
point resulting in a static scheme being somewhere in between simply supported and fixed. SteFi has 
the ability to calculate the load-bearing capacity of beams with fixed ends, but does not take the 
thermal expansion of the beam into consideration. Furthermore, no plastic benefit is gained when a 
beam with fixed ends is loaded at midspan with a point load, as the solicitant moment at midspan and 
at the ends are identical.  
Comparing the temperature independent imperfection presented in the Swedish literature (Pettersson, 
et al., 1976) to the calculation method prescribed in the Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007), revealed 
a reduction in insulation for the temperature independent imperfection. The hindered expansion factor 
was set to 1.0, meaning no hindering of expansion, to solely compare the difference between the 
imperfections.  
Investigating the impact of the deterioration factor, the results from the analysis reveals that there is 
only a tiny difference between the amount of insulation required when using Hertz’ deterioration 
factor for 2.0 % stress or the one prescribed by the Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007). However, 
there is a huge difference when the amount of insulation required for 0.2 % stress is compared to that 
of 2.0 %. This was expected based on the findings in Section 3.3, where the comparison of the graphs 
for the deterioration factors showed a higher reduction of the strength and stiffness for Hertz’ 
==PAGE:108==
 
90                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
deterioration factor for 0.2 % stress. An increase of 41.2 % is substantial, though not as much as the 
decrease in required insulation when the DS Parametric fire was compared to the ISO834 Standard 
fire. 
This leads up to a discussion of the third project objective formulated as: 
“Investigate the conventional resistance class design method and its applicability” 
Investigating the resistance class design method has been done stepwise for different configurations 
in the previous part. However, these investigations cannot conclude on the overall design method on 
their own. Doing so, requires a setup where a combination of configurations is investigated to see the 
combined effect of one design method over another.  
When the reduction factor for 0.2 % stress and the DS Parametric fire are combined, the amount of 
insulation required is still significantly reduced compared to the method prescribed by the Eurocode 
(DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007). The delayed heating of the steel caused by the insulation, and the brief 
duration of the DS Parametric fire, forms a self-perpetuating effect where a small increase in thickness 
has a great effect on the maximum steel temperature. This stresses the importance of the cooling 
phase of a fire, which the ISO834 Standard fire does not account for, and results in a great reduction 
of the amount of required insulation for this structure, even if the more conservative 0.2 % stress is 
used. This shows that designing the insulation based on the prescribed procedure in the Eurocode 
(DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) results in an over dimensioned fire protection in this case. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:109==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
91 
 
7 Conclusion 
 
This thesis presents the development and test of a BIM integrated software for an automated design 
and verification process for steel elements exposed to fire, and a comparison of different design 
approaches. This has been achieved by developing a fully functional stand-alone calculation software, 
SteFi, and an Autodesk Revit integration module, which can interact and manipulate with data in a 
BIM model. The design and verification process is based on the procedure prescribed by the Eurocode 
(DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) with the option for alternatives regarding fire development, deterioration 
factor, and verification method. The performance of the developed software, the impact of the 
alternate calculations, and the integration with BIM is investigated with a case study, to showcase the 
abilities of the developed software. The following sections will summarise the key findings and topics 
for further work. 
 
7.1 Highlights 
The developed software is separated into a calculation module and an integration module. The 
calculation module is developed in pure Python code as a compiled .exe file for Windows operating 
system without an installer, which makes it independent of the installed version of Windows. The 
calculation module is created with a GUI package called Tkinter and can be used with or without the 
integration module. The integration module is developed for Revit as Dynamo scripts which can run 
through the Dynamo Player function directly inside Revit. The two modules interact via an exchange 
file, either as .xlsx or .csv, which contains the geometrical data of the element to be verified or 
designed. 
The BIM integration and the abilities of the calculation module has been tested on a case study of a 
steel car-park with a R60 resistance class. The calculation module has been validated against a data 
set from another source on three parameters; steel heating, critical temperature, and insulation 
thickness. The calculation module is deemed to be within an acceptable margin as the difference 
between the performed calculations and the compared data set is 0.26/0.78 %, 1.65 %, and 5.36 % 
respectively. The difference in the temperature could be caused by the different calculation methods 
used to find the cross-section perimeter, and the difference in insulation thickness caused by different 
==PAGE:110==
 
92                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
thermal conductivities for the insulation. To ensure the validity of the results of the calculation 
module, external sources should be consulted before SteFi is used in a real-life application. 
The case study showed that the different variables for the insulation design have a huge impact on 
the total amount of insulation required for the car-park to obtain a R60 resistance class or resist a DS 
Parametric fire. Five scenarios were considered for eight different elements and compared to the one 
scenario prescribed by the Eurocode (DS/EN 1993-1-2, 2007) based on the required amount of 
insulation. The five scenarios included the impact of each option for each setting, and the combined 
effect of the DS Parametric fire and Hertz’ deterioration factor for 0.2 % stress. The largest difference 
in amount of required insulation occurred when only the fire type differed, with a reduction of 58.1% 
when the DS Parametric fire were used compared to the ISO834 Standard fire. The largest increase 
in amount of insulation occurred when the deterioration factor for 0.2 % stress were compared to the 
one for 2.0 % stress. The scenario with the DS Parametric fire and the deterioration for 0.2 % stress 
resulted in a reduction of 40.9 %, which shows that the conventional design approach based on 
resistance classes, in this case, results in an over-dimensioned insulation design. With such a large 
reduction, there is a huge economic intent to use a performance-based insulation design. With further 
development on the BIM integration module, the parameter values necessary for the fire model could 
be extractable, which would increase the potential of the software and enhance the design process. 
 
7.2 Further Studies 
Working on integrating structural fire design in a BIM environment has been an iterative process of 
developing a tool/design process focussing on giving the user freedom to choose between different 
BIM platforms, calculation settings, file formats etc. However, certain limitations have been 
necessary to draw a line between initial goals and the actual achievable outcome. This has both been 
to the extent of which parts were possible to implement within the given timeframe and which parts 
that could be left out without compromising the effect of the integration process. Setting a limitation 
does not imply that an option cannot be achieved, but it is merely a choice of improving other aspects 
of the project and leaving room for further development and improvements to the overall BIM 
integration process. 
A range of possible improvements and additions have been considered, based on the current limits of 
SteFi, the limits of the BIM integration, and the data exchange process. These are divided in the 
categories as follows. 
==PAGE:111==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
93 
 
SteFi specific Improvements 
The developed calculation software, at its current level, is a good basis for further development, 
whether this is with regards to graphical improvements, input options, calculations etc. The GUI is 
developed on a simple platform with no fancy graphical setup which reflects a technical layout. 
However, improvements could be made to the overall layout and arrangement of input/output data to 
give a more holistic experience when using the program. 
On the technical side, various adjustments and additions could be made to the calculations to give the 
user even more flexibility when investigating different verification and design possibilities. The 
following list contains some suggestions to improvements and additions to the program in its current 
version: 
• Extended variety of fire exposure types: 
The program only provides two options for choosing fire exposure and it would be beneficial 
to be able to calculate with exposure types such as the EN Parametric fire curve, Hydrocarbon 
fire curve, external fire curves, localised fires etc. 
• Extended list of predefined insulation materials: 
Currently, only a few types of insulation materials have been included in the program where 
many other types could be beneficial to use, depending on a given scenario and economical 
limitations. 
• Implementation of a standard profile library: 
The program currently takes cross-section data for three different profile types. Additional 
profile types could be added to include for example C-profiles, L-profiles and custom profiles. 
In addition to this, implementing a library for each profile type could make the selection a lot 
easier and all cross-section dimensions and data such as section modulus and moment of 
inertia would in this case be known by the program. By having a library of cross-sections, the 
design process could be enhanced dramatically both for non-fire situations and in fire 
situations. As the program determines the required insulation thickness based on a given fire 
design load, a similar process could run simultaneously testing different cross-sections sizes 
to find the optimal design for that given load. The downside of having a predefined library is 
that the data in the library may differ from what is extracted from a given BIM software, and 
will therefore give conflicting results. 
==PAGE:112==
 
94                                                        Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements 
 
• Improved settings for support conditions: 
As of now, the program provides eight different static schemes for beams and three for 
columns. Choosing between predefined static schemes is a limitation that should be avoided 
if the software should be developed even further. Instead, the user should be able to input the 
fixation data for the end nodes (end points) of an element by the means of the degrees of 
freedom at each node. By doing this, all possible support conditions can be covered, and the 
sectional forces would be calculated based on the given conditions. Furthermore, this method 
would make it possible to calculate the actual degree of rotation between elements connected 
in a frame in terms of the spring value based on the relative stiffness of connecting elements. 
• Include thermal expansion of beams: 
The theory for including thermal expansion for columns has previously been described and 
compared to known data. This comparison resulted in great differences for the load-bearing 
capacity of the columns. Therefore, the option for including thermal expansion have been 
disabled in SteFi. The thermal expansion theory should be investigated more thoroughly in 
the future to achieve better results when comparing with the Swedish literature. Including the 
thermal expansion for beams as well, will make the design process more holistic and could be 
investigated alongside the theory for the columns.  
 
A more general but extensive addition that could be made to the program is the verification and design 
of composite sections of steel and concrete. This would be a large upgrade to the current version that 
solely focuses on steel elements. Composite sections are a lot more complex in terms of verification 
but are widely used in the industry and is therefore an obvious choice when it comes to defining new 
goals for future development.   
 
BIM Integration Process Improvements 
To showcase an entire BIM integration process, Autodesk Revit was chosen as platform for extracting 
data from a model to be used in SteFi. Any other BIM program with built-in element data could have 
been chosen, and an integration module developed for that specific case. However, with focus on the 
Revit integration, some of the things that could be added and improved in the process are as follows: 
• Extraction and export of loads: 
==PAGE:113==
 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire Design of Steel Elements  
95 
 
As previously described, loads are one of the more difficult things to extract from a model 
and export to SteFi. Even though Revit does have options for setting up load cases, for 
example with area loads on slabs, it is rarely sufficient to assume that an even load distribution 
can be made between beams. But if investigation were to be made on this topic, even more 
data could be extracted for the given element to be used in the calculations. 
• Extraction and export of compartment properties: 
It would a great accomplishment if data for the compartment, in where an element is placed, 
could be extracted directly from the model. If a model is detailed to an extend where materials 
have been assigned to room boundaries and the interior equipment have been decided, all 
three parameters (O, b and q) for the natural fire models could be extracted. The strength of 
having this information is the immediate feedback a user can get for the fire design based on 
both architectural and structural choices which may change during a project. Furthermore, if 
this data is present in the model, the advantage of designing with natural fire models (deemed 
to be more accurate and reflect the actual fire that will occur) increases and may be more 
justified as a design method compared to the conventional resistance class design.

==PAGE:239==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Appendix G 
 
 
This appendix contains the user guide provided to understand the calculation module, SteFi. 
 
 
==PAGE:240==
1 
 
SteFi User Guide 
 
This document contains the user guide for the 1.0 beta version of SteFi and provides an overview of 
the different windows and settings in the program.  
Content 
 
General notes........................................................................................................................................ 1 
File-menu: ............................................................................................................................................ 1 
Settings-menu....................................................................................................................................... 2 
Help-menu:........................................................................................................................................... 2 
Cross-section data page ........................................................................................................................ 3 
Boundary conditions page .................................................................................................................... 4 
Fire type page ....................................................................................................................................... 5 
Calculations page ................................................................................................................................. 6 
Calculation options menu..................................................................................................................... 7 
Insulation data menu ............................................................................................................................ 8 
 
General notes 
For the program to work correctly the following must be respected: 
- 
Decimal numbers must be separated with a dot and not a comma 
- 
Any number must be entered in the specified unit 
The program is designed with four pages which contains different information required for the 
calculation to run, and three menus in the upper left corner which contains general functions not 
required for the calculation to run. 
 
 
File-menu: 
- 
New Project: This clears all data and resets options to default 
- 
Import Data: Here, data can be imported by selecting the file containing data exported form a 
                      BIM model. 
==PAGE:241==
2 
 
- 
Export Data: When the calculation is completed, the results can be exported from SteFi to an  
                      already existing .xlsx or .csv file by selecting this option. 
- 
Exit: This function terminates the program, which also can be done by pressing the cross in  
         the upper right corner. 
 
Settings-menu 
The Settings-menu contains only one option, which opens a pop-up window containing advanced 
settings with predefined values, which can be changed if wanted.  
 
1. The convective heat transfer is per default set to 35. 
 
2. The emissivity is divided into two parameters:  
- 
!" is the surface emissivity set to 0.7 as default. 
- 
!# is the emissivity of the flame set to 1.0 as default. 
 
3. The shadow effect from nearby elements is set to 1.0 as default and can be reduced to 
accommodate the shadowing from other elements.  
 
Help-menu: 
- 
About: This option opens the “About” file which contains information about the program. 
- 
User Guide: Selecting this option opens this exact user guide. 
- 
License agreement: Opens the “License agreement”. 
1 
2 
3 
==PAGE:242==
3 
 
Cross-section data page 
 
4. The different available cross-section types in the program can be selected from a predefined 
list in this drop-down menu which contains: 
- 
I-Profile 
- 
RHS  
- 
CHS  
 
5. Depending on the selected cross-section type, this field changes as the different cross-section 
types are described by different parameters. 
 
6. This image changes with the selected profile to illustrate the parameters for the selected profile 
type. 
 
 
4
5 
6 
7 
==PAGE:243==
4 
 
7. These input fields describe the material properties for the element, with predefined values for: 
- 
Stiffness with a default value of 210 GPa. 
- 
Steel density is set to 7850 kg/m3 as default. 
 
Boundary conditions page 
 
8. This is where the element type is selected and if “Beam” is selected, 12 is not displayed and 
14 is reduced to the first two options.  
 
9. Dependent on the choice of element type, this drop-down either shows eight pre-set boundary 
conditions for a beam, or three for a column. 
 
10. The length of the element should be inserted here. 
 
8 
10 
9
11 
12 
13 
14 
==PAGE:244==
5 
 
11. The load acting on the element is either inserted as a point load or as a uniformly distributed 
load, depending on the boundary conditions, with the appropriate unit always displayed.  
 
12. The choice here dictates the type of imperfection to use for the calculation. 
 
13. This image changes depending on what is selected in 9 to visualise the boundary conditions. 
 
14. The possible options for exposure type changes depending on the choice made in 8, and 
displays three different exposure types for a column and two for a beam. 
 
 
Fire type page 
 
15 
16 
17 
==PAGE:245==
6 
 
15. Here three different fire types can be selected which affects whether the entries in 16 are 
enabled or disabled. 
 
16. If the option for “No fire” is selected in 15, all entries are disabled. If “ISO834 Standard fire” 
is selected in 15, the resistance time is enabled. If “DS Parametric fire” is selected in 15, the 
three other entries are enabled and the resistance time disabled.  
 
17. Here the selected fire type in 15 with the inputs from 16 can be plotted and saved. 
 
 
Calculations page 
 
18. The program can either verify an element or design the insulation for an element, dependant 
on the choice here. If “VERIFICATION” is selected, all options in 20 is enabled whilst the 
option for no insulation and the “Thickness” are disabled if “DESIGN” is selected. 
18 
20 
19
21
22 
23 
==PAGE:246==
7 
 
 
19. Here the calculation is initiated by clicking on the “Calculate” button. A pop-up window with 
calculation options will occur if all required inputs are inserted, and an error message will 
occur if some data is missing. The pop-up calculation window can be seen in detail in the 
chapter “Calculation options menu”. Once the calculation is complete, the two other buttons 
are enabled and gives the possibility to plot the steel heating curve and print a report with the 
results for the calculation. 
 
20. Depending on the choice in 18, either all options are enabled if “VERIFICATION” is selected 
or the option for no insulation and the “Thickness” is disabled. The drop-down menu contains 
some pre-defined insulation materials which changes depending on the selection for either 
“Box encasement” or “Contour encasement”. It is possible to see the thermal conductivity and 
define a custom insulation material in 21. 
 
21. This button opens the pop-up window “Insulation data” where the thermal conductivity of the 
insulation materials can be inspected and a custom material can be defined. The pop-up 
window “Insulation data” can be seen in detail in the chapter “Insulation data menu”. 
 
22. When the calculation is completed, the calculated values for the cross-section is displayed 
here. 
 
23. The results from the calculation, apart from the cross-section data, are displayed here. The 
data varies depending on the selected options for the calculation. If “VERIFICATION” is 
selected in 18, the load bearing capacity is displayed, whilst the required insulation thickness 
is displayed if “DESIGN” is selected in 18. 
If any of the other pages are selected or any setting are changed, the results are erased as the 
may no longer be valid, but can be generated again but clicking the button “Calculate” in 19. 
 
Calculation options menu 
This menu appears as a pop-up window whenever the calculate button in 19 is pressed, and data is 
inserted in all entries required for the calculation. 
24. The factor used for the deterioration of the steel is selected here, and if the “Analytical” is 
selected, both options will be enabled for 25. Otherwise only the 2.0% option is enabled. 
 
25. Depending on the selection in 24, either both or only the 2.0% stress option is enabled. 
 
26. If the selected element is a beam, this is where either a sectional or an element analysis is 
selected for the calculation. If a column is selected as the element type, 26 is not displayed. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:247==
8 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Insulation data menu 
This pop-up window opens whenever the “Material data” button, 21, is clicked.  
 
27. Depending on the selected insulation type in 20, the drop-down list provides the same options 
as the drop-down list in 20. Whenever “Custom” is selected, the entry for the “Custom 
material name” is enabled. The custom material name will not appear anywhere in the 
program, but will be printed in the report and included in the data export.  
27
28
29
24 
25 
26 
==PAGE:248==
9 
 
28. The heat capacity ratio between the insulation material and steel can be inserted here, and is 
set to 0.0 as a default.  
 
29. The thermal conductivity properties for the material can be inserted for different temperatures 
if the “Custom” insulation material is selected. If any of the pre-defined materials are selected, 
the entries are disabled. 
 
 
==PAGE:249==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Appendix H 
 
 
This appendix contains the code report of the three files which together forms the program SteFi. 
The report consists of three parts, one for each of the thee files SteFi.py, BackEnd.py, and 
Report.py, with each part having separate page numbering. 
  
==PAGE:250==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Code Report 
Master of Science Thesis 
June 2018 
 
Master of Science in Architectural Engineering 
Mikkel Dohm Andersen - s123595 
Thomas Dyhr - s123569 
 
Supervisor  
Luisa Giuliani – Associate professor, Department of Civil Engineering, 
                           Technical University of Denmark 
Automatic and BIM-Integrated Fire 
Design of Steel Elements 
==PAGE:251==
==PAGE:252==
 
 
 
Content 
This is the code report of the program SteFi developed as a part of a master’s thesis and contains the 
entire code for the program. The code is separated in three files, each with its own report 
documenting the coding in the following order: 
Part I: Front-end code for the program (SteFi.py) 
Part II: Back-end code (BackEnd.py) 
Part III: Report generation code (Report.py) 
 
 
==PAGE:253==
==PAGE:254==
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Part I: SteFi.py

==PAGE:256==
